Definition of al-Murīd: The Attribute of Will
Sanusi defines al-murīd and argues for its necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-murīd and why God must be described with will.

Core Idea
Will is the attribute that tips the scale for one possible over another.

In this passage, Sanusi establishes that God is willing by showing that without will, the specific existence of things would be impossible. He then defines what it means to be willing. Let's examine the text carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'الله تعالى مريد' up to 'وهما محالان'. This argument shows the necessity of God's will.
Notice the logic: if not willing → no specification → impossible consequences.
2
Understand the definition of al-murīd
Read the definition that follows. It defines al-murīd as one who has an attribute that preponderates one possible over another.
The alternative wording 'القاصد' emphasizes intentionality.
3
Connect to the necessity of divine will
Reflect on how the definition flows from the conditional argument: if God were not willing, no specification, but the world is specified, so God must be willing.
This establishes will as a necessary attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله تعالى مريد
ومريداً ، وإلا لَمَا اختصصت بوجود ولا مقدار ولا صفة ولا زمن بدلاً عن نقائضها الجائزة ، فيلزمُ إِمَّا قدمُكَ ، أو استمرار عدمِكَ ، وهما محالانِ
المريدُ : هو مَنْ لهُ صفةٌ يُرجُحُ بها وقوع أحد طرفي الممكن ، وإن شئت فقل : هو القاصد لوقوع أحد طرفي الممكن ، ونظم الدليل على أنَّهُ تعالى مريد - لكن على غيرِ النظم الذي أشرنا إليه في العقيدة ـ أن نقول : الله جل وعلا خصَّصَ الحادث بأحد الطرفين الجائزين عليه
وكلٌّ مَنْ كانَ كذلك فهو مريدٌ ؛ فينتجُ : الله جل وعلا مريد .
أما الصغرى : فواضحةٌ ؛ إذ لا يخفى أنَّهُ لَمَّا كانَ وجود الممكناتِ وعدمُها بالنسبة إليها سواءً ؛ لا يجب أحدهما ولا يستحيل ، بل هما جائزان على السواء ، ثم إِنَّهُ جلَّ وعلا أوجد هذا الممكن.. فبالضرورة أنَّهُ تعالى هو الذي خصَّهُ بأحد الطرفين الجائزين عليه ؛ وهو الوجود ، ولم يبقِهِ على الطرف الآخَرِ الجائز ؛ وهو العدمُ ، وكذا أوجده على مقدارٍ مخصوص في ذاتِهِ ، فخصَّهُ أيضاً بذلك بدلاً عن الطرفِ الآخَرِ الجائز ؛ وهو أنْ يكونَ أكبرَ مِنْ ذلكَ المِقدارِ أو أصغر ،
وكذا خصه بالوجودِ في ساعة كذا مِنْ يوم كذا في شهر كذا في سنة كذا ، وكذا ما يتعلق الوجودِ المتقدم على ذلكَ أوِ المتأخّرِ
بدلاً
عن
God Most High is Willing. And if He were not willing, then you would not be specified with existence, nor with a particular quantity, nor quality, nor time, instead of their possible opposites. Then either your eternity would follow, or the continuation of your non-existence, and both are impossible. Al-murīd is the one who has an attribute by which the occurrence of one of the two possibilities of the possible is preponderated. And if you wish, say: it is the one who intends the occurrence of one of the two possibilities of the possible. And the formulation of the proof that He Most High is willing—but not according to the formulation we indicated in the creed—is that we say: God, may He be exalted and glorified, specified the originated thing with one of the two possible alternatives upon it. And everyone who is like that is willing; therefore, God, may He be exalted and glorified, is willing. As for the minor premise: it is obvious, because it is not hidden that when the existence of possibles and their non-existence are equal in relation to them—neither is necessary nor impossible, but both are equally possible—then He, may He be exalted and glorified, brought this possible into existence... So necessarily He Most High is the one who specified it with one of the two possible alternatives upon it, namely existence, and did not leave it upon the other possible alternative, namely non-existence. And likewise He brought it into existence with a specific quantity in its essence, so He specified it also with that instead of the other possible alternative, which is that it be larger or smaller than that quantity. And likewise He specified it with existence in a particular hour of a particular day in a particular month in a particular year, and likewise whatever pertains to existence preceding that or subsequent.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage begins with a heading stating that God is willing (murīd), followed by a conditional statement: if He were not willing, you would not be specified with existence, quantity, quality, or time, leading to the impossible consequences of your eternity or perpetual non-existence. Then it provides a definition of al-murīd: the one who has an attribute by which one of the two possibilities of a possible thing is preponderated; alternatively, the one who intends the occurrence of one of the two possibilities. Next, it presents the syllogistic proof: God specified the originated thing (al-ḥādith) with one of the two possible alternatives; everyone who does that is willing; therefore, God is willing. The author notes that this formulation differs from the one previously indicated in the creed. He then explains the minor premise: it is obvious because possibles are equally possible in terms of existence and non-existence—neither is necessary or impossible. God created this possible, so He necessarily specified it with existence rather than non-existence. Similarly, He specified it with a specific quantity rather than being larger or smaller, and with a specific time rather than another. The explanation is cut off at the end of the page, but the logical structure is clear: God's specification of possibles proves His will.

Key Terms
المريد
طرفي الممكن
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will implies change or desire similar to humans.
Correction: The definition shows that will is an eternal attribute that preponderates one possible over another, not a reactive desire.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if God were not willing?
A) The world would not exist.
B) You would not be specified with existence, quantity, quality, or time.
C) You would be eternal.
D) God would change.

Key Takeaway
God's will is the attribute by which He specifies contingent things with their particular characteristics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is what specifies every detail of your existence, from your time of birth to your current circumstance, are you living in submission to that will, or do you feel entitled to a different plan? How does that affect your trust in God's choices for you?
?
The argument shows that without God's will, you would not even exist in this precise moment. Does your daily life reflect an awareness that every breath is a manifestation of His specific will? What would change if you truly internalized that each detail is preponderated by His attribute?
?
You are reading this lesson at this exact time and place. Could it be otherwise? According to this passage, it could not, because God's will necessitated it. Do you accept that His will is always for your good even when you don't see the wisdom? Can you name one area where you struggle to accept that God's will is best?

Syllogistic Proof that God is Willing
Sanusi presents a syllogism and explains its minor premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the formal syllogism that proves God's will from the specification of possibles.

Core Idea
The specification of possibles is the rational proof for God's will.

After defining will, Sanusi presents a formal syllogistic proof that God is willing. The proof rests on the observation that contingent things are specified in existence, quantity, and time, which requires an attribute of will. Let's follow the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the syllogism
Read the Arabic from 'ونظم الدليل' to the end. Identify the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion.
The syllogism is: God specified the originated thing; everyone who specifies is willing; so God is willing.
2
Understand the major premise
The major premise is that God specified the originated thing (al-ḥādith) with one of two alternatives. Recognize that this is observable.
3
Understand the minor premise
The minor premise is that everyone who specifies is willing. Sanusi explains this by noting that possibles are equal, so specification cannot be without a will.
This premise is argued to be obvious (wāḍiḥa).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion that God is willing follows necessarily. Reflect on how specification implies will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله تعالى مريد
ومريداً ، وإلا لَمَا اختصصت بوجود ولا مقدار ولا صفة ولا زمن بدلاً عن نقائضها الجائزة ، فيلزمُ إِمَّا قدمُكَ ، أو استمرار عدمِكَ ، وهما محالانِ
المريدُ : هو مَنْ لهُ صفةٌ يُرجُحُ بها وقوع أحد طرفي الممكن ، وإن شئت فقل : هو القاصد لوقوع أحد طرفي الممكن ، ونظم الدليل على أنَّهُ تعالى مريد - لكن على غيرِ النظم الذي أشرنا إليه في العقيدة ـ أن نقول : الله جل وعلا خصَّصَ الحادث بأحد الطرفين الجائزين عليه
وكلٌّ مَنْ كانَ كذلك فهو مريدٌ ؛ فينتجُ : الله جل وعلا مريد .
أما الصغرى : فواضحةٌ ؛ إذ لا يخفى أنَّهُ لَمَّا كانَ وجود الممكناتِ وعدمُها بالنسبة إليها سواءً ؛ لا يجب أحدهما ولا يستحيل ، بل هما جائزان على السواء ، ثم إِنَّهُ جلَّ وعلا أوجد هذا الممكن.. فبالضرورة أنَّهُ تعالى هو الذي خصَّهُ بأحد الطرفين الجائزين عليه ؛ وهو الوجود ، ولم يبقِهِ على الطرف الآخَرِ الجائز ؛ وهو العدمُ ، وكذا أوجده على مقدارٍ مخصوص في ذاتِهِ ، فخصَّهُ أيضاً بذلك بدلاً عن الطرفِ الآخَرِ الجائز ؛ وهو أنْ يكونَ أكبرَ مِنْ ذلكَ المِقدارِ أو أصغر ،
وكذا خصه بالوجودِ في ساعة كذا مِنْ يوم كذا في شهر كذا في سنة كذا ، وكذا ما يتعلق الوجودِ المتقدم على ذلكَ أوِ المتأخّرِ
بدلاً
عن
God Most High is Willing. And if He were not willing, then you would not be specified with existence, nor with a particular quantity, nor quality, nor time, instead of their possible opposites. Then either your eternity would follow, or the continuation of your non-existence, and both are impossible. Al-murīd is the one who has an attribute by which the occurrence of one of the two possibilities of the possible is preponderated. And if you wish, say: it is the one who intends the occurrence of one of the two possibilities of the possible. And the formulation of the proof that He Most High is willing—but not according to the formulation we indicated in the creed—is that we say: God, may He be exalted and glorified, specified the originated thing with one of the two possible alternatives upon it. And everyone who is like that is willing; therefore, God, may He be exalted and glorified, is willing. As for the minor premise: it is obvious, because it is not hidden that when the existence of possibles and their non-existence are equal in relation to them—neither is necessary nor impossible, but both are equally possible—then He, may He be exalted and glorified, brought this possible into existence... So necessarily He Most High is the one who specified it with one of the two possible alternatives upon it, namely existence, and did not leave it upon the other possible alternative, namely non-existence. And likewise He brought it into existence with a specific quantity in its essence, so He specified it also with that instead of the other possible alternative, which is that it be larger or smaller than that quantity. And likewise He specified it with existence in a particular hour of a particular day in a particular month in a particular year, and likewise whatever pertains to existence preceding that or subsequent.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage begins with a heading stating that God is willing (murīd), followed by a conditional statement: if He were not willing, you would not be specified with existence, quantity, quality, or time, leading to the impossible consequences of your eternity or perpetual non-existence. Then it provides a definition of al-murīd: the one who has an attribute by which one of the two possibilities of a possible thing is preponderated; alternatively, the one who intends the occurrence of one of the two possibilities. Next, it presents the syllogistic proof: God specified the originated thing (al-ḥādith) with one of the two possible alternatives; everyone who does that is willing; therefore, God is willing. The author notes that this formulation differs from the one previously indicated in the creed. He then explains the minor premise: it is obvious because possibles are equally possible in terms of existence and non-existence—neither is necessary or impossible. God created this possible, so He necessarily specified it with existence rather than non-existence. Similarly, He specified it with a specific quantity rather than being larger or smaller, and with a specific time rather than another. The explanation is cut off at the end of the page, but the logical structure is clear: God's specification of possibles proves His will.

الصغرى
الطرفان الجائزان
تخصيص الحادث
الممكن

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the minor premise 'everyone who specifies is willing' considered obvious?
A) Because specification requires knowledge.
B) Because possibles are equal in themselves, so specification requires a will.
C) Because God is all-powerful.
D) Because the syllogism is self-evident.

Key Takeaway
The specification of possibles in existence, quantity, and time is the rational proof that God is willing.

Definition of al-Mureed and Proof that Allah is Willing
Sanusi defines al-mureed and provides a rational proof for divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-mureed and the rational proof that Allah is al-Mureed, based on the need for a preponderator for contingent specifications.

Core Idea
Allah's will is proven by the necessity of a preponderator for any contingent specification.

Sanusi begins by defining the term 'al-mureed' (the Willing) and then constructs a rational proof that Allah possesses this attribute. The proof hinges on the observation that contingent things are equally possible in multiple states, yet they are realized in one specific state, which demands a preponderator—identified as divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-mureed
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the two equivalent definitions: one focusing on an attribute that preponderates, the other on intention.
The definition establishes what it means for an agent to be 'willing'.
2
Understand the syllogistic structure
Note that the proof is presented as a syllogism: a major premise, a minor premise, and a conclusion. Identify each part as you read.
This logical structure is common in kalam arguments.
3
Examine the minor premise
Read the section beginning with 'أما الصغرى' which explains that Allah specifies each contingent being in its existence, measure, time, and attributes, thereby selecting one equally possible side over the other.
This premise is based on observable reality.
4
Examine the major premise
Read the section beginning with 'وأما بيان الكبرى' which argues that preponderating one of two equal sides without a preponderator is impossible, that the possible itself cannot be the preponderator, and that the external preponderator must be the will.
This is the core rational argument against self-sufficiency of contingent things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المريدُ : هو مَنْ لهُ صفةٌ يُرجُحُ بها وقوع أحد طرفي الممكن ، وإن شئت فقل : هو القاصد لوقوع أحد طرفي الممكن ، ونظم الدليل على أنَّهُ تعالى مريد - لكن على غيرِ النظم الذي أشرنا إليه في العقيدة ـ أن نقول : الله جل وعلا خصَّصَ الحادث بأحد الطرفين الجائزين عليه وكلٌّ مَنْ كانَ كذلك فهو مريدٌ ؛ فينتجُ : الله جل وعلا مريد . أما الصغرى : فواضحةٌ ؛ إذ لا يخفى أنَّهُ لَمَّا كانَ وجود الممكناتِ وعدمُها بالنسبة إليها سواءً ؛ لا يجب أحدهما ولا يستحيل ، بل هما جائزان على السواء ، ثم إِنَّهُ جلَّ وعلا أوجد هذا الممكن.. فبالضرورة أنَّهُ تعالى هو الذي خصَّهُ بأحد الطرفين الجائزين عليه ؛ وهو الوجود ، ولم يبقِهِ على الطرف الآخَرِ الجائز ؛ وهو العدمُ ، وكذا أوجده على مقدارٍ مخصوص في ذاتِهِ ، فخصَّهُ أيضاً بذلك بدلاً عن الطرفِ الآخَرِ الجائز ؛ وهو أنْ يكونَ أكبرَ مِنْ ذلكَ المِقدارِ أو أصغر ، وكذا خصه بالوجودِ في ساعة كذا مِنْ يوم كذا في شهر كذا في سنة كذا ، وكذا ما يتعلق الوجودِ المتقدم على ذلكَ أوِ المتأخّرِ بدلاً عن الألوانِ وسائرِ الأعراض خصَّهُ بنوعِ مِنْ ذلكَ بدلاً عن تركِهِ إلى مقابله . وأما بيانُ الكبرى : فلان ترجيح وقوع أحد الطرفين المستويين بغيرِ مرجح .. محال ، ويستحيل أن يكون المرجُحُ نفس ذلك الممكنِ ؛ لأنَّهُ يلزمُ عليهِ أنْ يكونَ مساوياً لذاتِهِ راجحاً لذاته ، وأيضاً فلأنَّهُ إِنْ تربَّحَ لَهُ مِنْ ذاتِهِ الوجود . . كانَ واجب الوجودِ لذاتِهِ ، فيلزم قدمُهُ ، وإنْ ترجّحَ لَهُ مِنْ ذاتِهِ العدمُ .. وجبَ استمرار عدمِهِ ، فلا يُوجَدُ أبداً ؛ لأنَّ المرجّحَ الذاتي يستحيلُ عدمُهُ ، وكلا القسمين باطل ، فتعيَّنَ أَنْ يكون المرجّح خارجاً عنهُ مِنْ جهة فاعله ، والسبرُ يقتضي أنْ لا مُرجّحَ لاختصاص الممكن بأحد الجائزاتِ عليه بدلاً عن مقابله.. إلا الإرادة ؛ وهي قصد الفاعل إلى فعل ذلك الجائز ، وإِن شئتَ قلتَ : اختياره له .
Al-mureed: he who has an attribute by which the occurrence of one of the two sides of the possible is preponderated. And if you wish you may say: he who intends the occurrence of one of the two sides of the possible. The formulation of the proof that He, exalted, is willing — but not according to the formulation we indicated in the creed — is that we say: Allah, majestic and sublime, specified the originated thing with one of the two permissible sides regarding it, and whoever is like that is willing; so it results: Allah, majestic and sublime, is willing. As for the minor premise: it is clear; for it is not hidden that since the existence of possibles and their non-existence are equivalent relative to them — neither is necessary nor impossible, rather both are equally permissible — then He, majestic and sublime, brought this possible into existence… so necessarily He, exalted, specified it with one of the two permissible sides regarding it, namely existence, and did not leave it on the other permissible side, namely non-existence. And likewise He brought it into existence on a specific measure in its essence, so He specified it with that instead of the other permissible side, namely that it be larger or smaller than that measure. And likewise He specified it with existence in a specific hour of a specific day of a specific month of a specific year, and likewise regarding the existence preceding that or succeeding it. And as for the major premise: its explanation is that preponderating the occurrence of one of two equal sides without a preponderator is impossible. And it is impossible that the preponderator be that possible itself; because that would entail that it is equal to itself and preponderant over itself. Also, because if existence is preponderated for it from itself, it would be necessary of existence by itself, so its eternity would follow; and if non-existence is preponderated for it from itself, then it would necessitate its perpetual non-existence, so it would never exist; because the self-preponderator cannot cease. Both divisions are false. So it is determined that the preponderator must be external to it from the agent's side. And the survey (السبر) entails that there is no preponderator for the specification of the possible with one of the permissible [sides] instead of its opposite except the will (الإرادة); which is the agent's intention to do that permissible [act], and if you wish you may say: his choice of it.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a rational proof. It begins by defining al-mureed (the willing one) as one who has an attribute by which one of the two possible states of a contingent thing is preponderated over the other. An alternative phrasing is 'one who intends the occurrence of one side.' The author then presents the proof that Allah is mureed. The proof is structured as a syllogism: (1) Allah specified the originated thing (al-hadith) with one of its two permissible sides. (2) Whoever does that is willing. Therefore, Allah is willing. The minor premise is explained by observing that all contingent things are equally possible in existence and non-existence, yet Allah brings them into existence at specific times, with specific measures, specific qualities, etc., thereby specifying them with one side rather than the other. The major premise is established by arguing that preponderating one of two equal sides without a preponderator is impossible. Moreover, the preponderator cannot be the contingent thing itself, as that would imply self-equality and self-preponderance, leading to either necessary existence (if existence preponderates from itself) or perpetual non-existence (if non-existence preponderates), both of which are false. Hence the preponderator must be external, and the only plausible candidate is the agent's will (al-irada), defined as the intention or choice of the agent to do that particular act.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof for divine will depends on temporal creation alone.
Correction: The proof actually relies on the need for a preponderator for any specification among equally possible alternatives, not merely on creation in time.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is necessary for one of two equally possible states to occur?
A) The possible itself must preponderate its own existence.
B) There must be an external preponderator.
C) The two states must be necessary.
D) The possible must be created in time.

Key Takeaway
The proof for Allah's will is grounded in the rational principle that any specification among equally possible alternatives necessitates an external preponderator, which is the divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The proof shows that without an external preponderator, nothing could be specified. Do you live each moment conscious that your very existence is sustained by Allah's will every second? What would change in your day if you truly believed that your next breath is only by His irada?
?
Chance is not a real cause; every event has a preponderator. When was the last time you attributed something to 'luck' or 'coincidence'? How can you train yourself to see the will of Allah behind every seemingly random event, starting today?

Refuting Power as the Preponderator
Sanusi refutes the claim that divine power alone can preponderate one possibility over another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power (qudra) cannot be the preponderator for bringing a possible into existence, and why divine will is necessary to specify which possible occurs and when.

Core Idea
Power is indifferent to all possibles; only will can specify.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a potential objection to the necessity of divine will in creation. Someone might argue that God's power (qudra) alone could determine why one possible exists rather than another. Sanusi's rebuttal is incisive: power relates equally to all possibilities and all times, so it cannot pick out a specific one without a prior specifying factor—namely, the will. Let's trace his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi first presents the objection (fain qulta) and then gives the answer (al-jawab).
The objector suggests power as the preponderator.
2
Identify the key premise
Focus on the sentence: 'power's relation to all possibles is one relation.' This is the crucial point: power is uniform, not selective.
Uniformity means no inherent preference.
3
Follow the time argument
Sanusi uses the example of time: all times are equal to eternal power. So power cannot explain why this specific time is chosen. There must be a preponderation by the agent (will) for this time.
Time specification proves the need for will.
4
Apply the analogy
The argument concludes by extending the reasoning to every possible: existence over non-existence must also be preponderated before power attaches. Think of other examples to test the logic.
The analogy solidifies the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : لعلَّ المرجّحَ لوقوع أحد الطرفين صفةُ القدرةِ . فالجواب : أنَّ القدرة نسبتها إلى جميع الممكنات نسبة واحدة ، فما بالها تعلقت بإيجاد هذا الممكن على الخصوص بدلاً عن مقابله ، وفي هذا الزمانِ المخصوص بدلاً عن المتقدم والمتأخّرِ ، والأزمانُ كلُّها بالنسبة إلى القدرة القديمة سواء ؟! فلا بدَّ إِذا مِنْ ترجيح الفاعل هذا الزمان للفعل ، وحينئذٍ يُوجِدُ بقدرتِهِ الفعل فيه ، وكذا لا بدَّ أنْ عنِ العدمِ ، ثم تتعلَّقُ بهِ القدرة ، وقس على ذلكَ كل ممكن .
If you say: Perhaps the preponderator for the occurrence of one of the two sides is the attribute of power (القدرة). The answer: That power's relation to all possibles is one relation, so why has it attached to bringing this particular possible into existence instead of its opposite, and at this particular time instead of the earlier or later? And times are all equal relative to the eternal power?! So there must be a preponderation by the agent of this time for the action, and then He brings the action into existence by His power in it. And likewise it is necessary that [He preponderates] existence over non-existence, then power attaches to it. And apply this analogically to every possible.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector proposes that divine power (qudra) itself could be the preponderator that specifies one possible over another. The author rebuts by pointing out that power is equally related to all possible things—it does not inherently favor one over another. Therefore, power alone cannot explain why a specific possible is brought into existence at a specific time rather than another. The element of time is used to illustrate: all times are equal relative to eternal power. Hence, there must be a prior preponderating factor, namely the will (irada), which determines the particular time and the particular existence. Then the power attaches to that chosen act. The argument concludes that this reasoning applies analogically to every possible contingent thing.

Key Terms
القدرة
الترجيح
المرجح
الإرادة
الممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that omnipotence alone can determine which possible occurs and when, without need for divine will.
Correction: Sanusi shows that power is indifferent—it relates equally to all possibles and all times. Therefore, a separate specifying principle (will) is necessary to explain why this particular possible exists now rather than its opposite or at another time.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi reject the idea that power (qudra) alone could be the preponderator for the existence of a possible?
A) Because power is not a real attribute.
B) Because power has a single relation to all possibles, so it cannot explain particular specification.
C) Because power is temporal.
D) Because power is separate from the divine essence.

Key Takeaway
Divine power is equally related to all possibles; therefore, divine will is the preponderator that specifies which possible exists and when.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that your own power or ability is enough to accomplish something, without aligning your intention to what God wills? How does Sanusi's argument remind you that even God's power is not deployed without a purpose?
?
In your daily choices, do you act as though time and possibilities are random, or do you recognize that every moment is deliberately prepondered by God? How would that reorientation affect how you plan your day?
?
If divine power is indifferent to all possibilities, but your will is often attached to many desires, how can you train your will to follow what God has already prepondered for you, rather than chasing what power alone could bring?

Proof That the Particularizer Is the Will
Sanusi argues that the particularizer of contingent existence must be the divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that a contingent being cannot be its own particularizer and must be particularized by the divine will.

Core Idea
No contingent thing can bring itself into existence; only the will of the Creator can tilt the scales.

Sanusi now presents a formal proof that the particularizer of a contingent's existence cannot be the contingent itself. He first establishes the impossibility of an event occurring without a particularizer, then demonstrates the absurdities of self-particularization. Finally, he identifies the only possible particularizer as the divine will. This argument forms the rational foundation for the attribute of will.

Guided Path
1
Major Premise: Necessity of a Particularizer
When two outcomes are equally possible, one cannot occur without something that tips the balance. This is the foundation of the argument.
Sanusi calls this impossibility 'محال' (impossible) — a rational impossibility.
2
Refutation of Self-Particularization
If the contingent itself were the particularizer, it would either prefer existence from itself (making it necessary and eternal) or prefer non-existence (making it perpetually nonexistent). Both are absurd and contradict its contingent nature.
Self-particularization leads to logical contradictions.
3
Conclusion: The Particularizer Is External Will
Since the contingent cannot particularize itself, the particularizer must be external — specifically, the will (irada) or choice of the agent. This is the only viable explanation for why a contingent exists in one state rather than another.
The will is defined as the agent's intention or choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالألوانِ وسائرِ الأعراض خصَّهُ بنوعِ مِنْ ذلكَ بدلاً عن تركِهِ إلى مقابله . وأما بيانُ الكبرى : فلان ترجيح وقوع أحد الطرفين المستويين بغيرِ مرجح .. محال ، ويستحيل أن يكون المرجُحُ نفس ذلك الممكنِ ؛ لأنَّهُ يلزمُ عليهِ أنْ يكونَ مساوياً لذاتِهِ راجحاً لذاته ، وأيضاً فلأنَّهُ إِنْ تربَّحَ لَهُ مِنْ ذاتِهِ الوجود . . كانَ واجب الوجودِ لذاتِهِ ، فيلزم قدمُهُ ، وإنْ ترجّحَ لَهُ مِنْ ذاتِهِ العدمُ .. وجبَ استمرار عدمِهِ ، فلا يُوجَدُ أبداً ؛ لأنَّ المرجّحَ الذاتي يستحيلُ عدمُهُ ، وكلا القسمين باطل ، فتعيَّنَ أَنْ يكون المرجّح خارجاً عنهُ مِنْ جهة فاعله ، والسبرُ يقتضي أنْ لا مُرجّحَ لاختصاص الممكن بأحد الجائزاتِ عليه بدلاً عن مقابله.. إلا الإرادة ؛ وهي قصد الفاعل إلى فعل ذلك الجائز ، وإِن شئتَ قلتَ : اختياره له .
By colors and all accidents, He specified it with a type of that instead of leaving it to its opposite. As for the demonstration of the major premise: because the preference for the occurrence of one of the two equal sides without a particularizer is impossible, and it is impossible that the particularizer be that same contingent; because it would then entail that it be equal to itself and preferable by itself, and also because if existence were preferred for it from its essence, it would be necessary of existence in itself, so its pre-eternity would be necessary; and if non-existence were preferred for it from its essence, its non-existence would necessarily persist, so it would never exist, because the essential particularizer cannot cease. Both divisions are false, so it is established that the particularizer must be external to it from the aspect of its agent; and investigation requires that there is no particularizer for the specification of a contingent with one of the possible states instead of its opposite except the will, which is the agent's intention to perform that possible act, and if you wish you may say: its choice.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the particularizer (murajjih) for a contingent being's existence cannot be the contingent itself and must be the divine will (irada). The author begins by referencing an example (colors and accidents) where a specific type is chosen over others. He then states the major premise: it is impossible for one of two equal [possible] sides to occur without a particularizer. Next, he refutes the possibility that the particularizer could be the contingent itself. If the contingent were its own particularizer, it would either prefer existence or non-existence from itself. If existence, it would be necessary of existence and thus eternal; if non-existence, it would never exist because the essential particularizer cannot cease. Both are false for a contingent being. Therefore, the particularizer must be external, specifically from the agent. The author concludes that the only external particularizer for specifying a contingent with one possible state is the will (irada), defined as the agent's intention or choice.

Key Terms
مرجح (murajjih)
إرادة (irada)
ممكن (mumkin)
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud)
ترجيح (tarjih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a contingent being could cause itself to exist or particularize itself without external cause.
Correction: The proof shows that if it did, it would either be necessary and eternal (contradicting its contingency) or never exist, which is false for existing things.

Checkpoint
Why cannot the contingent itself be its own particularizer?
A) Because it would lead to an infinite regress
B) Because it would result in either necessary existence or perpetual non-existence
C) Because it would contradict God's power
D) Because it would make the contingent equal to God

Key Takeaway
The divine will is the only adequate explanation for why a contingent being exists in one state rather than another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You heard that without God's will nothing can be determined. In what area of your life are you still trying to be your own particularizer, thinking you can tilt the scales yourself? Write one specific area where you need to submit to God's will today.
?
If this proof is true, then every detail of your life is willed by God. How does that truth change the way you view your past mistakes? Can you accept that even your failures are part of God's plan?
?
Have you ever thought you could bring yourself into existence or determine your own worth? This lesson shows that is impossible. How does this affect your reliance on your own power to achieve what you want? What would it mean to truly rely on God's will in your daily decisions?

Why Divine Power Alone Cannot Be the Particularizer
Sanusi refutes power as particularizer via objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of power (qudra) cannot serve as the particularizer (murajjih) and why the will (irada) is necessary to specify which possible is created and when.

Core Idea
Power is indifferent; will is what specifies.

In this passage, Sanusi presents and refutes a potential objection: perhaps the attribute of power itself is what particularizes one possible over another. The refutation hinges on a crucial insight: divine power relates equally to all possibles and all times. Therefore, power alone cannot account for why this particular thing exists now rather than something else. The will of Allah must be the source of specification.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the claim being proposed: that power (qudra) might be the particularizer. Note the phrase 'If you say: Perhaps the particularizer... is the attribute of power.'
This is a common line of thought that needs to be addressed.
2
Understand the core argument in the answer
Focus on the statement that power's relation to all possibles is one and the same. Ask yourself: if power is equally capable of creating any possible at any time, how could it single out one?
This is the heart of the refutation.
3
Trace the logical necessity
Notice that Sanusi concludes 'there must be a preference by the agent for this time for the act.' This shows that will (irada) is needed before power acts. Power then creates in accordance with will.
The order is: will specifies, then power executes.
4
Connect to the standard definition
Read the final sentence: 'Power is an expression for the attribute that has effect in accordance with the will.' This confirms that power follows will.
This definition encapsulates the lesson's conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : لعلَّ المرجّحَ لوقوع أحد الطرفين صفةُ القدرةِ . فالجواب : أنَّ القدرة نسبتها إلى جميع الممكنات نسبة واحدة ، فما بالها تعلقت بإيجاد هذا الممكن على الخصوص بدلاً عن مقابله ، وفي هذا الزمانِ المخصوص بدلاً عن المتقدم والمتأخّرِ ، والأزمانُ كلُّها بالنسبة إلى القدرة القديمة سواء ؟! فلا بدَّ إِذا مِنْ ترجيح الفاعل هذا الزمان للفعل ، وحينئذٍ يُوجِدُ بقدرتِهِ الفعل فيه ، وكذا لا بدَّ أنْ عنِ العدمِ ، ثم تتعلَّقُ بهِ القدرة ، وقس على ذلكَ يُربِّحَ الوجود بدلاً جع كل ممكن . ولهذا يقولون : القدرة عبارةٌ عنِ الصفة المؤثرة على وَفْقِ الإرادة .
If you say: Perhaps the particularizer for the occurrence of one of the two sides is the attribute of power. The answer: Power's relation to all possibles is one and the same; why then has it attached to bringing this particular possible into existence instead of its opposite, and in this particular time instead of before or after, while all times are equal with respect to eternal power?! So necessarily there must be a preference by the agent for this time for the act, and then He brings the act into existence by His power in it; and likewise there must be [preference for existence over] non-existence, then power attaches to it. And similarly for every possible. For this reason they say: Power is an expression for the attribute that has effect in accordance with the will.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector suggests that perhaps the attribute of power (qudra) itself could be the particularizer. The answer begins by noting that divine power has a uniform relation to all possible things; it is equally capable of creating any possible at any time. Thus, if power alone were the particularizer, it would have no reason to specify one possible over another or one time over another. Therefore, there must be a prior act of preference (tarjih) by the agent regarding the time and the specific possible. This preference is the will (irada). After this preference, power then creates in accordance with the will. The author then cites the well-known definition: power is the attribute that effects in accordance with the will, confirming that power follows will, not vice versa.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
إرادة (irada)
ترجيح (tarjih)
فاعل (fa'il)
زمان (zaman)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's power alone determines what He does, independent of any other attribute.
Correction: Power is indifferent; it is the will that specifies which possible is created and when. Power then executes the will's determination.

Checkpoint
Why is divine power alone insufficient to be the particularizer?
A) Because power is limited in scope
B) Because power is equal in relation to all possibles and times
C) Because power cannot create without will
D) Because power is not a real attribute

Key Takeaway
Divine power alone cannot particularize because it is equally related to all possibles and times; the will of Allah is necessary to specify which possible is created and when.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your own power (ability, resources, skills) can accomplish anything without first seeking Allah's will? How does this lesson's insistence that power alone cannot particularize challenge your assumption that you can achieve anything on your own steam?
?
Think of a major decision you are facing. Have you truly submitted to Allah's will (irada) regarding the outcome, or are you relying on your own ability (qudra) to make it happen? What would it look like to sincerely prefer Allah's choice over your own?
?
The passage says power 'effects in accordance with the will.' In your daily actions, is your will aligned with what Allah wills? If you fear a certain outcome, are you acknowledging that Allah's will may differ from yours, and are you ready to accept that?

Refutation of Knowledge as the Particularizer
Sanusi refutes that knowledge can particularizer, confirming will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge cannot be the particularizer of contingent events and how this refutes the objection, affirming that will is the true particularizer.

Core Idea
Knowledge reveals reality; will brings it into existence.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses an objection: could God's knowledge itself be what determines when and how a contingent thing occurs? After all, it's impossible for something to happen contrary to what God knows. Sanusi's answer draws a crucial distinction between attributes that affect reality and those that merely reflect it. Understanding this distinction is key to seeing why the will, not knowledge, is the particularizer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening objection carefully. The opponent claims that knowledge's connection to a possible's occurrence at a specific time and state could be the particularizer, because occurrence contrary to God's knowledge is impossible.
Notice how the opponent uses impossibility of opposition as a rationale.
2
Identify the answer's key move
In the answer, Sanusi distinguishes between affecting attributes and non-affecting attributes. Specification is an effect, so only an affecting attribute can cause it.
This distinction is central to the refutation.
3
Find the evidence
Sanusi provides evidence that knowledge is non-affecting: it attaches to the necessary (God) and the impossible, which cannot be affected by knowledge.
Think about what it means for knowledge to 'attach' to something without changing it.
4
Follow the elimination
After establishing knowledge as non-affecting, only power and will remain as affecting attributes. But power's role in specification has already been refuted in a previous lesson.
This eliminates all candidates except will.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that will is the particularizer. Consider how this preserves the doctrine of divine will's efficacy.
The conclusion is stated as 'and that is what was sought'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : لعل المرجّحَ تعلُّق العلم بوقوع ذلك الممكن في الزمن المخصوص على الصفة المخصوصة ؛ لأنَّ وقوع الممكن على خلافِ علم الله مستحيل . قلنا : التخصيص للممكن بالزمن المخصوص والصفة المخصوصة تأثير فيه بإيقاع بعض الجائزاتِ عليه ، فلا يتعلّق بهما إلا الصفةُ المؤثرةُ ، والعلمُ لَيسَ مِنَ الصفاتِ المؤثرة ؛ بدليل تعلقِهِ بالواجب والمستحيل ، فلم يبق إلا القدرة والإرادة ، وقد بطل بما سبق تعلق القدرةِ بالتخصيص ، فتعيَّنَ أنَّ المتعلّق بذلك الإرادة ، وهو المطلوب .
If you say: Perhaps the particularizer is knowledge's connection to the occurrence of that possible in the specific time and specific state; because the occurrence of a possible contrary to God's knowledge is impossible. We say: The specification of the possible with the specific time and specific state is an effect in it by bringing about one of the possible states upon it, so only the affecting attribute can be attached to them. Knowledge is not among the affecting attributes, as evidenced by its connection to the necessary and the impossible. So nothing remains except power and will, and the connection of power with specification has already been refuted by what preceded. Thus it is established that the one attached to that is the will, and that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents another objection and its answer. The objector claims that God's knowledge (ilm) of a possible's occurrence at a specific time and in a specific state could be the particularizer, since the occurrence of a possible contrary to God's knowledge is impossible. The answer distinguishes between affecting attributes (sifat mu'athira) and non-affecting attributes. Specification of a possible involves bringing about one of its possible states—this is an effect or action. Therefore, it can only be attributed to an affecting attribute. Knowledge, however, is not affecting; it only reveals or catches the object as it is. Evidence is that knowledge attaches to the necessary (God) and the impossible, which are not affected by it. Therefore, the only affecting attributes are power and will. But power's role in specification has already been refuted (in the previous lesson), so only will remains. Thus the particularizer is the will, which is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
علم ('ilm)
صفة مؤثرة (sifa mu'athira)
تخصيص (takhsis)
إرادة (irada)
قدرة (qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's foreknowledge is what causes events to happen as they do.
Correction: Divine knowledge only knows events; it does not bring them about. The will is the active particularizer.

Checkpoint
Why cannot divine knowledge be the particularizer of a contingent?
A) Because knowledge only reveals and does not affect
B) Because knowledge is not eternal
C) Because knowledge is limited to specifics
D) Because knowledge requires prior will

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge does not cause events; it only knows them as they are, while the will brings about their specific realization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If knowledge alone does not cause events, then your own knowledge of right and wrong does not make you act rightly. Where have you been deceived into thinking that knowing the truth is the same as living it? What one step will you take this week to move from knowledge to action?

The Preponderant Factor is the Will, Not Knowledge
Sanusi defines power and proves that the preponderant is will, not knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the preponderant factor in specifying a possible event is divine will, not knowledge.

Core Idea
Will is effective; knowledge only reveals.

This lesson examines a key argument in Islamic theology about divine attributes. Sanusi defines power as the effective attribute and demonstrates that the specification of possibilities is attributed to will, not knowledge. We'll follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the argument structure: definition, objection, refutation, conclusion.
2
Understand the definition of power and knowledge
Identify how Sanusi defines power (qudra) as the effective attribute in accordance with will, and knowledge as merely revealing without effect.
3
Analyze the objection about knowledge as preponderant
Consider the objection that perhaps knowledge's connection to a specific occurrence is the preponderant factor. Note why this seems plausible.
The objection relies on the impossibility of a possible occurring contrary to God's knowledge.
4
Follow the refutation and conclusion
See how Sanusi refutes the objection by pointing out that specification is an effect, so only an effective attribute can be connected to it. Knowledge is non-effective, proven by its connection to the necessary and impossible. Therefore, only will remains as the preponderant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا يقولون : القدرة عبارةٌ عنِ الصفة المؤثرة على وَفْقِ الإرادة . العلم صفة كاشفة ليسَ مِنْ شأنها التأثيرُ ] فإن قلت : لعل المرجّحَ تعلُّق العلم بوقوع ذلك الممكن في الزمن المخصوص على الصفة المخصوصة ؛ لأنَّ وقوع الممكن على خلافِ علم الله مستحيل قلنا : التخصيص للممكن بالزمن المخصوص والصفة المخصوصة تأثير فيه بإيقاع بعض الجائزاتِ عليه ، فلا يتعلّق بهما إلا الصفةُ المؤثرةُ ، والعلمُ لَيسَ مِنَ الصفاتِ المؤثرة ؛ بدليل تعلقِهِ بالواجب والمستحيل ، فلم يبق إلا القدرة والإرادة ، وقد بطل بما سبق تعلق القدرةِ بالتخصيص ، فتعيَّنَ أنَّ المتعلّق بذلك الإرادة ، وهو المطلوب
And for this reason they say: power (qudra) is an expression for the effective attribute in accordance with will (irada). Knowledge (ilm) is a revealing attribute, not among its concerns is effectiveness. If you say: perhaps the preponderant (murajjih) is the connection of knowledge to the occurrence of that possible (mumkin) in the specific time and specific manner, because the occurrence of the possible contrary to God's knowledge is impossible. We say: the specification (takhsis) of the possible with the specific time and specific manner is an effect on it by bringing about some of the contingencies upon it, so only the effective attribute can be connected to them. Knowledge is not among the effective attributes, evidenced by its connection to the necessary (wajib) and impossible (mustahil). So nothing remains except power and will, and it has been refuted by what preceded that power is connected to specification. Thus it is determined that the attribute connected to that is will, and that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of power (qudra) and then argues that the preponderant factor (murajjih) in the specification of a possible occurrence is will (irada), not knowledge (ilm). It begins by stating the definition: power is the effective attribute operating in accordance with will. Knowledge is merely a revealing attribute without effect. An objection is then posed: perhaps the preponderant is knowledge's connection to the occurrence in a specific time and manner, because the occurrence of a possible contrary to God's knowledge is impossible. The answer is that specification (takhsis) is an effect, hence it can only be attributed to an effective attribute. Knowledge is not effective, as proven by its connection to the necessary and impossible, which are not subject to effect. Thus only power and will remain. However, it has already been refuted that power is connected to specification, so the only remaining attribute is will. This is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
القدرة (qudra)
الإرادة (irada)
العلم (ilm)
المرجّح (murajjih)
التخصيص (takhsis)
الممكن (mumkin)
الواجب (wajib)
المستحيل (mustahil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine knowledge directly determines the occurrence of events.
Correction: Knowledge only reveals events without effecting them; the effective attribute is will.

Checkpoint
Why can the preponderant factor not be knowledge according to the passage?
A) Because knowledge is effective but limited.
B) Because knowledge is a non-effective attribute, as evidenced by its connection to the necessary and impossible.
C) Because knowledge only applies to possible things.
D) Because knowledge and will are the same.

Key Takeaway
The preponderant factor in specification is divine will, not knowledge, because will is effective while knowledge is only revelatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refuting the Mu'tazili Claim of Observing the Best
Sanusi refutes the claim that the preponderant is consideration of what is best.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazili claim that God acts for benefit is rejected and why it cannot serve as a preponderant.

Core Idea
God is not obligated to consider benefit; the claim is a Mu'tazili doctrine.

This passage addresses a key Mu'tazili argument that God's actions are motivated by creating benefit for creatures. Sanusi dismisses this as a sectarian claim and points to an upcoming proof that God is not obligated to consider what is best. The response shows why this cannot be the preponderant.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The passage begins with an objection: someone says the preponderant is benefit known to God. Read this and note that it represents the Mu'tazili position.
2
Understand the response
Sanusi calls it a Mu'tazili statement and says proof will come that God is not obligated to consider benefit. This is a dismissal and a reference to later proof.
3
See the conclusion
Because the claim is invalid, it cannot serve as a preponderant. Consider why this matters for understanding divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
1 إبطال أن يكون المرجح مراعاة الأصلح ] فإِنْ قلت : لقائل أنْ يقول : المرجُحُ لوقوع أحد الجائزينِ على المصلحة المعلومة لفاعله تعالى اشتماله قلنا : هذه مقالةٌ اعتزاليةٌ ؛ أعني : مراعاة المصلحة ، وسيأتي برهان عدمِ وجوب مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح في حقه تعالى(۱) ، وإذا بطل مراعاة المصلحة حتماً لم تصلح لترجيح الفعل بها .
1 Refuting that the preponderant is consideration of the most appropriate. If you say: someone might say that the preponderant for the occurrence of one of the two possibilities is its inclusion of the benefit (maslaha) known to its agent, Exalted is He. We say: This is a Mu'tazili statement – I mean consideration of benefit – and the proof of the non-obligation of considering what is good (salah) and what is best (aslah) in His right, Exalted, will come later(1). And when consideration of benefit is definitively invalid, it cannot serve as a preponderant for the act.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazili claim that the preponderant factor in the occurrence of an action is that the action contains benefit (maslaha) known to God. An objection is raised, and the answer dismisses it as a Mu'tazili statement. Sanusi then points to an upcoming proof that God is not obligated to consider what is good or best. Therefore, this claim is invalid and cannot serve as a preponderant.

Key Terms
مراعاة الأصلح (ri'ayat al-aslah)
المصلحة (maslaha)
اعتزالية (I'tizali)
الصلاح (salah)
الأصلح (aslah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God must always act for the best benefit of creation.
Correction: This passage shows that such a claim is a Mu'tazili doctrine and not obligatory; a proof will follow that God is not obligated to consider what is good or best.

C) Benefit is unknowable.
D) It contradicts divine will.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili claim that the preponderant is benefit is rejected because God is not obligated to consider it.

Only Allah is the True Free Agent
Sanusi argues that only Allah is the true free agent, refuting an objection about human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why only Allah qualifies as the true free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) and why human actions, though they appear to be freely chosen, are actually created by Allah.

Core Idea
Only Allah is the true free agent; human action is created by Allah, not produced by human power.

Sanusi now addresses a subtle objection: if will is necessary for free action, how can humans be considered free when they sometimes act unconsciously? His answer clarifies a crucial distinction between the agent who creates an action and the agent in whom the action occurs. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for understanding divine causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objector's challenge that humans perform actions without awareness, which seems to undermine the need for will in free agency.
2
Identify Sanusi's key distinction
Notice how Sanusi distinguishes between 'the agent who creates the action' (al-mujid) and 'the agent in whom the action occurs' (the human). The argument only applies to the creator.
This distinction is central to the entire argument.
3
Understand the role of human power (qudra)
The passage clarifies that the power we feel is itself a created attribute with no effect on the action; it is concurrent, not causal.
4
Apply the argument to the objection
See how Sanusi's distinction resolves the objection: the unconscious actions of humans do not disprove that only Allah is the true free agent, because humans are not the creators of those actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفاعلُ المختارُ الحقُّ هو الله تعالى ] فإن قلت : ما ذكر تُموهُ مِنْ أنَّ تخصيص أحد طرفي الممكنِ بالوقوع في حق المختارِ لا يكون إلا بصفة الإرادة.. ينتقص عليكم بالمختارِ منَّا ؛ فإنَّهُ يوقعُ أفعالاً في زمن مخصوص وعلى صفة مخصوصة وهو ذاهل عنها لا شعور لهُ بها ، فضلاً أنْ يقصد إليها ويريدها فالجواب : أنَّ كلامنا إِنَّما هو في المختارِ الموجِدِ للفعل . والمختارُ منَّا لا يُوجِدُ فعلاً أصلاً ، لا في حق نفسِهِ ولا في حق غيرِهِ ، وإِنَّما المُوجِدُ للذواتِ الحادثة وجميع أفعالها عموماً هو الله جل وعلا وسيأتي برهان ذلك في فضل خلْقِ الأعمال (۱) ، فالفعلُ إنَّما يُستدَلُّ باختصاصه بما اختَصَّ بهِ مِنَ الجائزاتِ على أنَّ فاعله الموجِدَ له - الله جل وعلا - مريدٌ ، لا على أنَّ فاعلَهُ ـ أي : الذي قام بهِ الفعلُ ، وأوجده الله تعالى فيهِ ، وهو الفاعلُ منَّا - مريدٌ ؛ لأنَّا لا نُوجِدُ شيئاً مِنْ أفعالنا ، بل الله جلَّ وعلا يُوجِدُها فينا ، إلا أَنَّهُ تارةً يُوجِدُها سبحانهُ ويُوجدُ معَها صفةٌ تُسمَّى قدرةً نُحِس بها تيسر ذلك الفعل علينا ، ولا تأثير لهذه القدرة في الفعل أصلاً ، بل هي مثلَهُ فعلُ اللهِ جل وعلا خُلِقَ مقارناً له (۲) ، وفي هذه الحالة التي يخلقُ اللهُ معَ الفعل
The true free agent is Allah, Exalted is He. If you say: What you have mentioned – that the specification of one of the two sides of the possible in the case of a free agent can only be by the attribute of will.. is contradicted by the free agent among us, for he performs actions in a specific time and in a specific manner while being heedless of them, having no awareness of them, let alone intending and willing them. The answer: Our speech is only concerning the free agent who creates (mujid) the action. The free agent among us does not create any action at all, neither with respect to himself nor with respect to another. Rather, the Creator of both essences that come into existence and all their actions generally is Allah, Mighty and Majestic. The proof of that will come later in the section on the creation of actions(1). So the action, by its being characterized by the specific possibilities with which it is characterized, only indicates that its creator—Allah, Mighty and Majestic—is willing, not that its agent—that is, the one in whom the action subsists and in whom Allah has created it, and that is the agent among us—is willing, because we do not create anything from our actions; rather, Allah, Mighty and Majestic, creates them in us, except that sometimes He creates them, and along with them He creates an attribute called power (qudra) through which we sense the facilitation of that action for us, and this power has no effect on the action at all, but rather it is like it—an act of Allah, Mighty and Majestic—created concurrently with it(2), and in this state in which Allah creates with the action...
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage establishes that the only true free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) is Allah. An objection is raised: the argument that specification requires will seems to fail because humans often perform actions unconsciously without intending them. The answer distinguishes between the agent who creates the action (al-mujid) and the agent in whom the action occurs. Humans do not create any action; Allah creates all actions and essences. So the specification of an action indicates that its Creator is willing, not that the human in whom it occurs is willing. Moreover, the power (qudra) that humans sense is itself a created attribute with no effect; it is merely concurrent with the action. Thus, the argument stands.

Misconception: Humans are truly free agents because they experience choosing and performing actions.
Correction: Humans do not create their actions; all actions are created by Allah. The experience of choice is accompanied by a created power (qudra) that has no effect.

According to the passage, what does the specification of an action indicate?
A) That the human agent is willing.
B) That Allah is willing.
C) That the action has intrinsic goodness.
D) That power has effect.

Key Takeaway

Refutation of the Objection on Human Actions and Kasb
Sanusi refutes an objection about human actions and explains the theory of kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that human actions prove human will, by distinguishing the true agent (God) from the human locus.

Core Idea
Only God creates and specifies actions; humans are merely containers.

This passage presents a key objection and its refutation regarding divine will and human action. The objector claims that human free agents sometimes act without will, challenging the link between will and specification. Sanusi responds by clarifying that humans do not create any act—God is the sole creator. The concept of kasb (acquisition) explains the human role.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objector's claim: that human free agents act without awareness or will, which would contradict the necessity of will for specification. Understand the challenge.
The objector is drawing from the same principle Sanusi uses.
2
Understand Sanusi's key distinction
Sanusi distinguishes between the true agent who creates the act (God) and the human who is merely a locus. The human does not create any act; only God creates and specifies.
This distinction is central to the entire refutation.
3
Learn the theory of kasb
Sanusi explains when a human is called 'agent' or 'chooser': it is terminological, only when God creates power (qudra) alongside the act. This power has no causal effect.
Kasb means acquisition, not creation.
4
Reflect on the container metaphor
The final paragraph summarizes that essences are like containers for acts; both container and contained are created by God. Reinforce the absolute exclusivity of divine causality.
This metaphor guards against any notion of independent human causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : ما ذكر تُموهُ مِنْ أنَّ تخصيص أحد طرفي الممكنِ بالوقوع في حق المختارِ لا يكون إلا بصفة الإرادة.. ينتقص عليكم بالمختارِ منَّا ؛ فإنَّهُ يوقعُ أفعالاً في زمن مخصوص وعلى صفة مخصوصة وهو ذاهل عنها لا شعور لهُ بها ، فضلاً أنْ يقصد إليها ويريدها
فالجواب : أنَّ كلامنا إِنَّما هو في المختار الموجِدِ للفعل . والمختارُ منَّا لا يُوجِدُ فعلاً أصلاً ، لا في حق نفسِهِ ولا في حق غيرِهِ ، وإِنَّما المُوجِدُ للذواتِ الحادثة وجميع أفعالها عموماً هو الله جل وعلا وسيأتي برهان ذلك في فضل خلْقِ الأعمال (1) ، فالفعلُ إنَّما يُستدَلُّ باختصاصه بما اختَصَّ بهِ مِنَ الجائزاتِ على أنَّ فاعله الموجِدَ له - الله جل وعلا - مريدٌ ، لا على أنَّ فاعلَهُ ـ أي : الذي قام بهِ الفعلُ ، وأوجده الله تعالى فيهِ ، وهو الفاعلُ منَّا - مريدٌ ؛ لأنَّا لا نُوجِدُ شيئاً مِنْ أفعالنا ، بل الله جلَّ وعلا يُوجِدُها فينا ، إلا أَنَّهُ تارةً يُوجِدُها سبحانهُ ويُوجدُ معَها صفةٌ تُسمَّى قدرةً نُحِس بها تيسر ذلك الفعل علينا ، ولا تأثير لهذه القدرة في الفعل أصلاً ، بل هي مثلَهُ فعلُ اللهِ جل وعلا خُلِقَ مقارناً له (2) ، وفي هذه الحالة التي يخلقُ اللهُ معَ الفعل قدرةً تقارنُهُ يُسمى العبد في الاصطلاح مختاراً ومكتسباً وفاعلاً ، وإلا سُمِّيَ مُضطراً ومجبوراً ، ثم قد يخلق الله سبحانَهُ معَ هذين الفعلينِ - وهما القدرة والمقدور - علماً للعبدِ وإرادةً لِمَا خلقَ فيهِ ، وتارةً لا يخلق له ذلكَ ، كما أنَّهُ تعالى معَ إفرادِهِ الفعل بالخلْقِ دون القدرة قد يخلقُ للعبدِ شعوراً بذلك ، وقد لا .
[ الذواتُ ظروف للأفعال المخلوقة فيها ]
وبالجملة : فالذواتُ كالظروفِ للأفعال المخلوقة فيها ، يخلق الله تعالى منها ما شاء وكيفَ شاءَ ، والظرفُ والمظروفُ فعلُ اللهِ تعالى ، لا تأثير لبعض في بعض ، فتباركَ مَنْ لا شريكَ لهُ في مُلْكِهِ ، ولا مُدبَّرَ معَهُ سواه ، ولا يُسأَل عما يفعل جلَّ وعلا .
If you say: 'What you have claimed – that the specification of one of two possible outcomes to occur on the part of the free agent (al-mukhtar) only occurs through the attribute of will (al-irada)... contradicts you concerning the free agent among us; for he performs actions at a specific time and in a specific manner while being heedless of them, having no consciousness of them, let alone intending them and willing them.' The answer: Our speech is only concerning the free agent who creates (al-mujid) the act. The free agent among us does not create any act at all, neither with respect to himself nor with respect to another. The creator of all originated substances and all their actions, universally, is God, Almighty and Sublime, and the proof of that will come in the chapter on 'creation of actions' (khalq al-a'mal) (1). The act is only inferred, by its specification to what it is specified with among possibles, that its creator (al-mujid) – God Almighty and Sublime – is willing (murid), not that its agent – i.e. that in which the act subsists and God created it in him, which is the agent among us – is willing; because we do not create anything from our actions, but rather God Almighty and Sublime creates them in us. However, sometimes He creates them, and with them is created a quality called power (qudra) which we sense facilitates that act for us, and this power has no effect on the act at all; rather, it is like it – an act of God Almighty created concurrent with it (2). In this state in which God creates with the act a power concurrent with it, the servant is terminologically called 'chooser' (mukhtar), 'acquirer' (muktasib), and 'agent' (fa'il); otherwise he is called 'constrained' (mudtar) and 'compelled' (majbur). Then God, glorified, may create with these two acts – namely power and the object of power (maqdur) – knowledge for the servant and will for what He created in him, and sometimes He does not create that for him, just as He, exalted, when He creates the act alone without power, may create for the servant consciousness of that, and may not. [Essences are containers for the acts created in them] In summary: essences are like containers for the acts created in them; God creates from them what He wills and how He wills. The container and the contained are both the act of God, exalted, with no effect of one upon another. So blessed is He who has no partner in His dominion, and no governor with Him besides Him, and He is not asked about what He does, Almighty.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (shubha wa jawab) concerning divine will. The objector argues: if will is necessary for specification in a free agent, then human free agents often act without awareness or will (e.g., involuntary movements), contradicting the claim that acts of free agents require will. Sanusi answers by distinguishing the true agent, who creates the act (God), from the human, who is merely a locus (mahall) for the act. Humans do not create any act; God is the sole creator of all substances and acts. The act's specification to a certain possibility rather than another indicates that its creator (God) is willing. The human is called 'agent' or 'chooser' terminologically only when God creates power (qudra) alongside the act, and this power has no causal effect. Sanusi then explains the theory of kasb (acquisition): the human is called muktasib when God creates power concurrent with the act; otherwise, the human is compelled. God may also create knowledge and will in the human, but not necessarily. The final paragraph summarizes: essences are like containers for acts; both container and contained are created by God alone. This guards against the misunderstanding that human choice implies independent causality alongside God.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المختار
الموجِد
القدرة
الكسب
الفاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human actions performed with deliberation prove human will as an independent cause.
Correction: Sanusi explains that humans do not create actions; God creates both the act and any accompanying power. The human is called 'agent' only terminologically, and the power has no causal effect. The true agent and cause of specification is God alone.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi argue that a human action performed without awareness does not disprove the necessity of divine will for specification?
A) Because humans are always aware of their actions, so the objection is false.
B) Because the human is not the creator of the act; only God creates and specifies acts.
C) Because unaware actions are not real actions.
D) Because God creates will in all humans at all times.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi defends the exclusivity of divine causality by showing that human agency is merely a locus for God's creation, and the term 'agent' is applied only when God creates power alongside the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action that you consider your own choice, do you genuinely acknowledge that God creates that action in you, or do you feel a sense of independence? How would your reliance on God change if you truly internalized that your power is created and ineffective?
?
If your 'choice' is merely a terminological label for when God creates power in you, what does that imply about your responsibility and accountability before God? Are you more or less accountable?
?

The Logical Proof for Divine Will from Specification
Sanusi presents a logical proof for divine will from specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses a conditional syllogism to prove that the specification of contingent beings necessarily implies the will of their Creator.

Core Idea
Specification of anything contingent requires a willing agent.

This lesson unpacks Sanusi's concise logical proof for the attribute of divine will. He takes a line from the matn—'otherwise you would not have been specified'—and expands it into a formal conditional syllogism. The proof demonstrates that without a willing agent, no contingent being could be selected for existence over its opposite. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, noting the key terms. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the technical terms like الاستثنائي and الملازمة.
2
Understand the conditional syllogism
Identify the 'if-then' structure (if agent not willing, then no specification). Recognize how Sanusi explains the entailment linking will to specification.
This is a qiyas istithna'i, a hypothetical syllogism.
3
See how specification proves will
Reflect on the conclusion: specification cannot occur without a specifier (mukhasis), which is the will. Therefore, our own existence proves Allah's will.
This proof is foundational in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإلا لَمَا اختَصَصْتَ ) إلى آخرِهِ : نظم الدليل على لفظِهِ مِنَ الاستثنائي ؛ وذلكَ أنْ يُقالَ : لو لم يكن فاعلُ ذاتِكَ مريداً لَمَا اختَصِصْتَ بوجود إلى آخره ، وبيانُ الملازمة : أَنَّكَ عرفت فيما سبق أن لا سبب لاختصاص الممكن ببعض ما جازَ عليه إلا إراده فاعلِهِ ، فإذا قُدِّرَ أَنَّ الفاعل غير مريد.. لزم استحالة وجودِ ممكن بعينه بدلاً عن مقابله ؛ ضرورة عدم الاختصاص عند عدم المخصص .
His statement: '(And otherwise you would not have been specified)' up to its end: the organization of the proof according to its wording is from the conditional (al-istithna'i). That is, one would say: If the agent of your essence were not willing, you would not have been specified with existence, etc. The clarification of the entailment: You have known previously that there is no cause for the specification of a possible to some of what is possible for it except the will of its agent. So if it is assumed that the agent is not willing, then the existence of a particular possible instead of its opposite becomes impossible; necessitating the absence of specification when the specifier (al-mukhasis) is absent.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof (dall al-istithna'i) for the necessity of divine will. It comments on a line from the matn: 'And otherwise you would not have been specified.' The proof is structured as a conditional syllogism: (1) If the agent of your essence were not willing, you would not have been specified with existence (i.e., you would not exist as this particular person). (2) The entailment is explained: you already know that the only cause for a possible thing to be specified with one of its permissible alternatives is the will (irada) of its agent. (3) Therefore, if the agent is assumed not to be willing, the existence of a specific possible rather than its opposite becomes impossible, because specification necessarily requires a specifier (mukhasis). Without the specifier (i.e., will), no specification occurs. This proof establishes that the existence of any contingent being implies the will of its creator. The passage is technical: it names the logical form (istithna'i, i.e., hypothetical syllogism) and explicitly links specification to will, a cornerstone of Ash'ari theology.

Key Terms
الاستثنائي
الملازمة
الممكن
اختصاص
المخصص
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Specification could arise from an impersonal cause (e.g., chance or nature).
Correction: Sanusi insists that only a willful agent can specify a possible into existence; without will, no specific possibility can be realized.

Checkpoint
What logical form does Sanusi attribute to this proof for divine will?
A) Categorical syllogism (qiyas hamli)
B) Conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i)
C) Induction (istiqra')
D) Analogy (tamthil)

Key Takeaway
The specification of any contingent being logically necessitates the will of its Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your very existence as this specific person, with your exact traits and circumstances, is not accidental but specifically willed by Allah. Do you live your daily life as if this were true? Name one area where you act as if your situation is random. What would change if you truly believed every detail was chosen for you? Commit to a concrete action today that reflects this belief.
?
The proof shows that nothing in your life happens without Allah's will—even the trials you dislike. Why do you often resist or complain about what He has specified for you? What does that resistance reveal about your trust in His wisdom? What will you do this week to surrender more fully to His choices for you?
?
If specification requires a willing Agent, then every blessing you have is a deliberate gift from Him. When was the last time you truly thanked Allah for specifying you to exist in this time, place, and condition? How can you cultivate a constant awareness throughout today that each moment is a chosen gift, not a random occurrence?
