The Proof of the Necessity of Divine Will through Specification
Sanusi presents a logical proof that a voluntary agent must have will, and refutes the alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that a voluntary agent must have will, based on the necessity of specification for possible beings.

Core Idea
Without divine will, no specific possible being could exist — an impossibility.

In this passage, Sanusi constructs a conditional syllogism to prove that Allah must possess the attribute of will. The argument begins from a simple premise: the existence of specific contingent things depends on a specification that only a willing agent can provide. By showing that denying this leads to absurd conclusions (either pre-eternity or eternal non-existence), the proof establishes the necessity of divine will.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional syllogism
Notice that the proof is structured as a conditional: 'If the agent were not willing, then specification would not occur.' This is the first step of the argument.
The Arabic term for this is استثنائي.
2
Grasp the entailment (mulāzama)
Read the explanation of why the conditional holds: without will, there is no cause for a possible being to be specified to one of its many possible states.
This is a key link in the argument.
3
Refute the consequent (buṭlān al-lāzim)
The proof then shows that the consequent (no specification) is false because specification is observed, and because without specification the possible would have to be either pre-eternal or non-existent, both impossible.
This completes the proof by reductio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإلا لَمَا اختَصَصْتَ ) إلى آخرِهِ : نظم الدليل على لفظِهِ مِنَ الاستثنائي ؛ وذلكَ أنْ يُقالَ : لو لم يكن فاعلُ ذاتِكَ مريداً لَمَا اختَصِصْتَ بوجود إلى آخره ، وبيانُ الملازمة : أَنَّكَ عرفت فيما سبق أن لا سبب لاختصاص الممكن ببعض ما جازَ عليه إلا إراده فاعلِهِ ، فإذا قُدِّرَ أَنَّ الفاعل غير مريد لزم استحالة وجودِ ممكن بعينه بدلاً عن مقابله ؛ ضرورة عدم الاختصاص [؟] المخصص عند عدم [؟] كونها مثل الفعل ، وقوله : ( فعلُ الله ) أي : مفعولة له ، وبالرفع : خبر ( هي ) ، وقوله : ( فعلُ الله بدل منه ؛ أي : إنَّ القدرة مماثلة للفعل في أنَّ كلاً منهما فعل الله . وأمَّا بطلان اللازم فبوجهين : أحدهما : مشاهدة الاختصاص في الممكناتِ والثاني : وهو ما أشار إليهِ في العقيدة مِنْ لزوم اتصافِ ذاتِ الممكن بأحد أمرين : وجوب القدم ، أو استمرار العدمِ ، وكلاهما محال فى حقِّهِ ؛ أمَّا الأَوَّلُ : فلِمَا مضى مِنْ برهان حدوثِ الممكناتِ كلها ، وأما الثاني : فلمشاهدة الوجودِ فيها . وبيانُ لزومِ أحدِ الأمرين عند تقدير عدم الاختصاص بممكن دونَ ممكن : أنَّ عدم الاختصاص بـالـوجـود وما يتبعُهُ مِنَ المقدارِ المخصوص والصفة المخصوصة يُوجِبُ استمرار العدم ، وعدم الاختصاص بالزمن المعيَّنِ يُوجِبُ القدمَ أو استمرار العدم ؛ لأنَّ الزمنَ لمَّا كانَ لا يَتَصفُ بهِ إلا المتجدِّدُ فلا ينتفي عنه إلا القديم أو المستمر العدم ؛ إذْ لا تجدُّدَ لهما ، فظهر أنَّ لزوم الاتصاف بأحدِ الأمرينِ عند عدم الاختصاص بتلك الأمور المذكورة يتعيَّنُ أحدهما ؛ وهو استمرار العدم فيما عدا الزمان ، ويلزم أحدهما لا بعينه في الزمانِ ، لكن لم يُفصِّلْ في العقيدة ؛ لأنَّه قصد ما يلزم في عدم الكلِّ مِنْ حيثُ هو كلٌّ ، لا ما يلزم في عدم كلّ واحدٍ . ويصح عطفُ قولِهِ : ( فيلزمُ إمَّا قدمُكَ . . . ) إلى آخرِهِ بالواو بدل ، وهو أحسنُ وأفيد ، ويكون دليلاً آخر مستقلاً بنفسِهِ معطوفاً على الأَوَّلِ ، ونظمه على هذا أَنْ يُقالَ : لو لم يكن فاعلُكَ مريداً . .
His saying: (And if not, you would not have been specified) to the end: the organization of the proof according to its wording from the conditional syllogism; that is, it is said: If the agent of your essence were not willing, you would not have been specified for existence etc. The explanation of the entailment: you knew in what preceded that there is no cause for the specification of a possible thing to some of what is possible for it except the will of its agent. So if it is assumed that the agent is not willing, then the existence of a particular possible thing instead of its opposite becomes impossible, due to the necessary absence of specification. [؟] the specifier when there is no [؟] its being like the act. And His saying: (act of God) i.e., something made by Him, and in the nominative: predicate of (it), and His saying: (act of God) is a substitute for it; i.e., the power is similar to the act in that each of them is an act of God. As for the invalidity of the consequent, it is through two ways: One of them: the observation of specification in possible things. And the second: that which he indicated in the Creed: the necessary attribution of the essence of the possible to one of two things: necessity of pre-eternity, or continuation of non-existence, both impossible regarding it. As for the first: due to what has passed of the proof of the temporal origination of all possible things. As for the second: due to the observation of existence in them. And the explanation of the necessity of one of the two things when assuming the absence of specification of one possible instead of another: that the absence of specification of existence and what follows it of particular measure and particular attribute necessitates the continuation of non-existence, and the absence of specification of the particular time necessitates pre-eternity or the continuation of non-existence; because time is only attributed to what is temporally originated, so nothing is free from it except the pre-eternal or the continuously non-existent, since there is no temporality for them. So it appears that the necessity of attribution to one of the two things when there is absence of specification of those mentioned matters entails one of them, which is continuation of non-existence in regards to other than time, and one of them without specification in time, but he did not detail in the Creed; because he intended what is necessary in the absence of all, insofar as it is a whole, not what is necessary in the absence of each one. And it is correct to coordinate his saying: (Thus necessarily either your pre-eternity... ) etc. with the letter 'and' instead, and it is better and more beneficial, and it becomes another independent proof conjoined to the first. Its organization according to this is that it is said: If the agent of you were not willing...
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof for the necessity of the divine will in the origination of any specific possible being. It is a conditional syllogism (istithnāʾī). The argument starts from the statement: 'If the agent of your essence were not willing, you would not have been specified for existence' (the Arabic is quoting a prior text). The logical entailment (mulāzama) is explained: earlier it was established that no cause for the specification of a possible thing to one of its many possible states exists except the will of its agent. Therefore, if we suppose the agent is not willing, then it is impossible for any specific possible thing to exist rather than its opposite, because there would be no specification (takhṣīṣ). The consequent of this conditional is rejected as false through two arguments (wajhān). First, specification is empirically observed in possible things (e.g., one being exists while another does not). Second, and more elaborate, is the argument from the Creed (al-ʿAqīda): if there were no specification, then the possible thing would necessarily be described by one of two impossible things—either it would be pre-eternal (qadīm) or it would be continuously non-existent (mustamir al-ʿadam). Both are impossible: pre-eternity is refuted by the proof of temporal origination (ḥudūth), and continuous non-existence is refuted by the observable existence of things. The passage then explains why the absence of specification leads to one of these two: if a possible thing has no specification to existence, it would remain non-existent continuously; if it has no specification to a particular time, it would have to be either pre-eternal (since time only applies to originated things) or continuously non-existent. In matters other than time, continuous non-existence is determined; in time, either pre-eternity or continuous non-existence results, but in the Creed the author (al-Sanūsī) did not differentiate because he considered what is necessary for the absence of the whole (the entire set of possibilities), not for each part individually. Finally, an alternative wording is suggested: instead of 'Then necessarily either your pre-eternity...' being a result, it could be coordinated as another proof, giving another conditional: 'If your agent were not willing, then...'.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof is circular or that it presupposes the existence of a will.
Correction: The proof starts from a premise that the opponent already accepts: that without will, specification cannot occur. It then shows the absurd consequences (either pre-eternity or non-existence) to force the conclusion that will must exist.

Checkpoint
What are the two ways to refute the consequent (bāṭil al-lāzim) in this proof?
A) Observation of specification and the argument from necessary attributes of the possible.
B) The impossibility of pre-eternity and the impossibility of continuous non-existence.
C) The proof of temporal origination and the observation of existence.
D) Both A and B.

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that the existence of specific contingent beings requires a willing creator, refuting any notion of a non-willing agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that without God's will, you would not exist in your specific form, at this exact time, with your particular attributes. Do you truly live as if every detail of your existence is directly willed by Him, or do you sometimes attribute your specific circumstances to chance or your own effort? Reflect on one area of your life where you have not acknowledged His will, and commit to recognizing it today.
?
The consequence of denying God's will is either pre-eternity (impossible) or non-existence. You are alive and specific, so you cannot deny His will. How does this logical certainty affect your trust in Allah's decree? Will you now accept His decisions over your life without complaint?
?
Sanusi argues that specification is empirically observed—look around you. The specific place you sit, the precise health you have, the exact moment you are reading this. Can you honestly say you see these as signs of a Willing Creator, or do you take them for granted? What will you do today to increase your awareness of Allah's will in your daily affairs?

The Agent Must Be Willing: An Independent Argument
Sanusi argues that an unwilling agent leads to absurdities, so the Agent must be willing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of contingent beings requires that their Creator be a willing agent with free choice, not a mere necessary cause.

Core Idea
A contingent world cannot arise from a blind cause; only a willing Agent explains its existence.

This lesson presents Sanusi's second argument for the attribute of divine will. Independently from the previous proof, it shows that if the Creator were not willing, either the universe would be eternal or it would never exist. The argument proceeds from the opponent's premise that the agent is not willing and demonstrates the absurd consequences, forcing the conclusion that the Agent must be willing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Note the alternative phrasing with 'waw' instead of 'fa'.
The text notes that both readings are valid; we follow the independent argument version.
2
Understand the two horns of the dilemma
Identify the two possibilities: either the existence of the contingent is necessitated by the agent's essence/attributes, or it is not. Each leads to an absurd conclusion (eternity of the contingent or perpetual non-existence).
The term 'ملازمة' (entailment) is key – the proof shows why these follow logically.
3
Reflect on the concept of 'specifying factor'
Focus on the phrase 'ترجيح بلا مرجح'. Without a specifying factor, no time, quantity, or quality can be preferred, leading to perpetual non-existence. This shows the need for a willing agent who chooses.
This is a classic kalam argument against 'blind' necessary emanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصح عطفُ قولِهِ : ( فيلزمُ إمَّا قدمُكَ . . . ) إلى آخرِهِ بالواو بدل الفاء ، وهو أحسنُ وأفيد ، ويكون دليلاً آخر مستقلاً بنفسِهِ معطوفاً على الأَوَّلِ ، ونظمه على هذا أَنْ يُقالَ : لو لم يكن فاعلُكَ مريداً . . لَلَزِمَ إِمَّا قدمُكَ ، أو استمرار عدمِكَ ، وبيانُ الملازمة : أنَّ الفاعل إذا لم يكن مريداً ؛ فإنْ كانَ وجود الممكن لازماً لوجوده أو لوجودِ صفةٍ مِنْ صفاتِهِ ؛ بحيثُ لا يحتاجُ في وجودِ ذلك الممكن إلى قصد .. لزمَ قدمُ ذاتِكَ ، وقدمُ سائرِ الممكناتِ ؛ لاستحالة وجودِ الملزوم بدونِ اللازم ، وقد مرَّ وجوبُ القدم لفاعلِكَ ولصفاتِهِ ، فما لزمهما يجبُ أَنْ يكون كذلك ، وإن لم يكن وجود الممكن لازماً لوجودِ ذاتِهِ ولا لوجودِ صفةٍ مِنْ صفاتِهِ .. لزمَ استمرار عدم ذاتِكَ وعدم سائر الممكناتِ ؛ لاستحالة ترجيح زمنٍ أو مِقدارٍ أو صفةٍ بلا مُرجح
And it is permissible to join his saying: (for what follows would be either your eternity... ) up to its end with the letter waw instead of fa', and that is better and more beneficial, and it would be another independent argument conjoined to the first. Its organization according to this is that one says: If your agent were not willing... then your eternity or your perpetual non-existence would be necessary. And the explanation of the entailment: that the agent, if not willing, then if the existence of the contingent were necessary by his existence or the existence of an attribute from his attributes, such that he does not need intention in the existence of that contingent... then the eternity of your essence and the eternity of all other contingents would be necessary, because of the impossibility of the existence of the subject without its necessary concomitant, and the necessity of eternity for your agent and his attributes has already passed, so what they necessitate must be likewise. And if the existence of the contingent were not necessary by the existence of his essence nor by the existence of an attribute of his attributes... then perpetual non-existence of your essence and the non-existence of all other contingents would be necessary, because of the impossibility of giving preference to a time, quantity, or quality without a specifying factor.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative phrasing of the argument that the agent must be willing. It begins with a textual note that the phrase 'فيلزم إما قدمك...' could be read with the conjunction 'wa' instead of 'fa', making it an independent argument coordinated to the first. The argument is then laid out: if your agent (the Creator) were not willing (i.e., lacks free choice), then one of two absurdities follows: either your essence (the contingent's essence) would be eternal, or it would perpetually fail to exist. The proof of this entailment (بيان الملازمة) is as follows: if the agent acts without will, then either the existence of the contingent is necessitated by the agent's essence or attributes, in which case the contingent would be co-eternal with the agent because a necessary concomitant (ملزوم) cannot be separated from its subject (لازم), and the agent and his attributes are already proven eternal; or the existence is not necessitated, in which case there would be no reason (مرجح) to prefer any particular time, quantity, or quality for the contingent's existence, so it would remain non-existent forever. Thus, the existence of a contingent world requires that its Maker be a willing agent.

مريد

استمرار العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes free will from the outset.
Correction: The argument actually begins with the opponent's assumption that the agent is not willing and shows that this leads to absurdities, thereby proving that the agent must be willing.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, if the agent is not willing and the existence of a contingent is not necessitated by the agent's essence or attributes, what follows?
A) The contingent's existence would be eternal
B) The contingent would be created by a different agent
C) The contingent would never exist (perpetual non-existence)
D) The contingent would exist at a specific time by accident

Key Takeaway
Without will, a cause either produces its effect necessarily or not at all – the contingent world shows neither is the case, so the Agent must be willing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your very existence at this moment is not the result of blind necessity but of God's specific will. Does your life reflect gratitude for that personal choice, or do you live as if your existence were accidental?
?
If God were a force without will, you would have no purpose, no response to prayer, no hope of guidance. How often do you truly rely on God's will in your daily decisions, and what would change if you stopped doubting that He personally chooses to care for you?
?
The argument shows that no ‘specifying factor’ other than will can explain why you exist now rather than not. Are you living with the weight of that gift, or are you treating your time as if it were infinite and unaccountable?

Why the Maker Cannot Be a Nature or Efficient Cause
Sanusi applies the previous argument to rule out nature and necessary causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maker of the world cannot be a nature or an efficient cause, and how the objection of hindrance or missing condition leads to either denying eternity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
A nature or necessary cause would make the world either eternal or impossible.

Sanusi continues his proof that the Maker must be a free agent (mukhtar) by eliminating two alternatives: nature and efficient cause. He shows that any attempt to explain delay through hindrance or condition only pushes the problem back, leading to an impossible consequence. This lesson follows the logical thread from the previous argument to its necessary conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the argument flow through you.
Notice the compact style of Sanusi.
2
Identify the two alternatives
Find where Sanusi states that the Maker cannot be a nature (طبيعة) or efficient cause (علة موجبة). Note the link to the previous argument about eternity or non-existence.
3
Trace the objection and response
The objection: a nature might delay its effect due to a hindrance or missing condition. The response: this leads to either denying eternity or infinite regress.
Consider why this response is effective.
4
Understand the definition of mukhtar
Sanusi reminds that the free agent (مختار) is one from whom both action and refrain can proceed, contrasting with nature which cannot refrain.
This definition is crucial for the overall argument.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The only remaining possibility is that the Maker is a free chooser. How does this align with Islamic theology?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان استحالة كون الصانع طبيعة أو علة موجبة.
فإنْ أُجيبَ عنِ التأخُرِ في الطبيعة بالمانع أو فوات الشرط .. لزم عدم القديم أو التسلسل ؛ لنقل الكلام إلى ذلك المانع وذلك الشرط
الإشارة بـ ( هنا ) راجعة إلى لزوم قدمِك أو استمرار عدمِكَ ؛ أي : بهذا اللازمِ يُستدَلُّ أيضاً على امتناع أنْ يكونَ صانع العالم طبيعةً أو عِلَّةٌ مُوجِبةً ، وقد عرفت فيما مضى أنَّ مَنْ يتأتّى منه الفعلُ والترْكُ يُسمَّى مختاراً ، ومَنْ لا يتأتّى منهُ التركُ إن لم يمكن أن يمنعه مانع مِنَ
Statement of the impossibility of the Maker being a nature or an efficient cause. For if it is answered about the delay in the nature due to a hindrance or missing condition... then the non-existence of the eternal or infinite regress would be necessary, because the speech would be transferred to that hindrance and that condition. The reference by (here) goes back to the necessity of your eternity or your perpetual non-existence; meaning: by this necessary consequence it is also used to prove the impossibility of the Maker of the world being a nature or an efficient cause. And you have already known in what preceded that whoever can do and refrain is called free (mukhtar), and whoever cannot refrain, if no hindrance can prevent him...
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage draws the theological conclusion that the Maker cannot be a nature (طبيعة) or an efficient cause (علة موجبة), both of which act without will. The same dilemma from the previous argument—either eternity or perpetual non-existence—is invoked to rule out these alternatives. Then an objection is considered: what if the delay in a nature's effect is due to a hindrance (مانع) or a missing condition (فوت شرط)? The response is that invoking a hindrance or condition merely postpones the problem, because one must then ask whether the hindrance or condition itself is eternal or temporal, leading to either denying eternity (if the hindrance is not eternal but created, then it cannot prevent the effect of an eternal nature) or infinite regress (تسلسل) (if the hindrance requires a previous hindrance, ad infinitum). Finally, Sanusi clarifies that the reference of 'here' is to the necessary consequence of the previous argument, which also serves to prove the impossibility of the Maker being a nature or efficient cause. He ends by defining the free agent (مختار) as one from whom both act and refrain can proceed, as opposed to a compelled agent who cannot refrain.

Key Terms

علة موجبة
مانع
شرط
تسلسل
مختار
عدم القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a nature can produce effects without will, and that delay can be explained by hindrances.
Correction: Sanusi's argument shows that invoking a hindrance or condition merely postpones the problem, leading to either denying the eternity of something necessary or infinite regress.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what consequence follows if one tries to explain the delay in a nature's effect by a hindrance or missing condition?
A) It proves the nature is all-powerful
B) It leads to either denying the eternity of something or infinite regress
C) It shows the nature is actually willing
D) It proves the effect is eternal

Key Takeaway
The Maker cannot be a nature or necessary cause; only a free agent (mukhtar) can account for the world's temporal existence without falling into eternal effect or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat Allah as if He is a natural force — expecting His mercy without turning to Him, or fearing His punishment without seeking His forgiveness? How does the fact that He is al-Mukhtar, free and personal, change the way you should relate to Him today?
?
If Allah were a necessary cause, your obedience would be automatic and your faith would be mechanical. But He is not. He chooses to respond to you. What does this mean for your sincerity and your du'a? Will you speak to Him today as a Person, not a force?
?
The objection of a hindrance or condition leads to infinite regress. In your own life, do you rely on excuses ('conditions') to delay your turning to Allah? What is the 'hindrance' you are hiding behind?

The Dilemma of an Unwilling Agent
Sanusi presents a conditional argument leading to two absurd alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an agent without will leads to two impossible alternatives: either all possible things are pre-eternal or they never exist.

Core Idea
An agent without will can only create either everything or nothing.

Sanusi begins by presenting a logical dilemma. If the Creator were not a willful agent, then either the existence of all possible things would be necessary and thus pre-eternal, or their existence would require a external preferential factor, leading to an infinite regress. This dilemma sets the stage for proving that the Creator must be willful.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Sanusi posits a conditional: if the agent lacks will, then either 'your pre-eternity' or 'continuation of your non-existence' follows.
The 'you' here refers to the student's essence as an example of a possible thing.
2
Identify the two cases
The explanation breaks into two cases: (1) if the possible's existence is necessary due to the agent, it must be pre-eternal like the agent; (2) if not necessary, a preferential factor is needed, without which the possible never exists.
Understand the concept of 'luzūm' (necessary entailment).
3
Absorb the absurdity
Both cases lead to conclusions that contradict observed reality (the world is originated). Therefore, the premise that the agent lacks will is false.

لَلَزِمَ إِمَّا قدمُكَ ، أو استمرار عدمِكَ ، وبيانُ الملازمة : أنَّ الفاعل إذا لم يكن مريداً ؛ فإنْ كانَ وجود الممكن لازماً لوجوده أو لوجودِ صفةٍ مِنْ صفاتِهِ ؛ بحيثُ لا يحتاجُ في وجودِ ذلك الممكن إلى قصد .. لزمَ قدمُ ذاتِكَ ، وقدمُ سائرِ الممكناتِ ؛ لاستحالة وجودِ الملزوم بدونِ اللازم ، وقد مرَّ وجوبُ القدم لفاعلِكَ ولصفاتِهِ ، فما لزمهما يجبُ أَنْ يكون كذلك ، وإن لم يكن وجود الممكن لازماً لوجودِ ذاتِهِ ولا لوجودِ صفةٍ مِنْ صفاتِهِ .. لزمَ استمرار عدم ذاتِكَ وعدم سائر الممكناتِ ؛ لاستحالة ترجيح زمنٍ أو مِقدارٍ أو صفةٍ بلا مُرجح . بيان استحالة كون الصانع طبيعة أو علة ] ومِنْ هنا تعلم استحالة كون الصانع طبيعةً أو عِلَّةً مُوجِبةً . فإنْ أُجيبَ عنِ التأخُرِ في الطبيعة بالمانع أو فوات الشرط .. لزم عدم القديم أو التسلسل ؛ لنقل الكلام إلى ذلك المانع وذلك الشرط . الإشارة بـ ( هنا ) راجعة إلى لزوم قدمِك أو استمرار عدمِكَ ؛ أي : بهذا اللازمِ يُستدَلُّ أيضاً على امتناع أنْ يكونَ صانع العالم طبيعةً أو عِلَّةٌ مُوجِبةً ، وقد عرفت فيما مضى أنَّ مَنْ يتأتّى منه الفعلُ والترْكُ يُسمَّى مختاراً ، ومَنْ لا يتأتّى منهُ التركُ إن لم يمكن أن يمنعه مانع مِنَ الفعل سُمِّيَ عِلَّةً ، وإن أمكن سُمِّيَ طبيعة . وبيانُ لزوم أحدِ الأمرينِ إِذا قُدَّرَ صانعُ العالم طبيعةً أو عِلَّةً : أَنَّ الطبيعة والعلَّةَ لا يخلو أن تكونا قديمتين أو حادثتين ، فإن كانتا قديمتين لزم قدمُ العالم ؛ لأنَّ فعل العلة والطبيعة إنَّما هو باللزوم لا بالاختيار، وقدمُ الملزوم يقضي بقدم لازمه ، وقد عرفت بالبرهان حدوث العالم ، وإن كانتا حادثتينِ افتقرتا إلى عِلَّةٍ أو طبيعة ، ودارَ أو تسلسل ، وقد سبق بيان ذلكَ عندَ بيان كون فاعلكَ قديماً ، والدورُ والتسلسل محالانِ على ما مضى ، فكونُ العلة والطبيعة حادثتين محال ، فوجود ذاتِكَ وسائرِ العالم الموقوفِ عليهما محال ، والمحالُ مستمر العدمِ ، فقد لزم استمرار العدمِ لذاتِكَ ولسائر العالم ! والعِيانُ يُكَذِّبُ ذلك . والحاصلُ : أَنَّهُ يلزمُ قدمُ العالمِ إِنْ فُرِضَتِ العَلَّةُ أَوِ الطبيعةُ قديمتين ، واستمرار عدمِهِ إِنْ فُرضتا حادثتين ، وكلا اللازمين باطل فالملزوم - وهو كون صانع العالم طبيعة أو عِلَّة - باطل ، فتعيَّنَ أَنْ يكون فاعلاً بالاختيار ، وهو المطلوب ، وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ ويختارُ [القصص : ٦٨] . ويلزم أيضاً على تقديرِ العلَّةِ أو الطبيعة قديمتين وجود ما لا نهاية له ؛ لأنَّ نسبة العلّة والطبيعة إلى الممكنات نسبة واحدة ، والممكناتُ لا نهاية لها ، فيلزم وجود جميعها دفعةً ، وهذا المحالُ في الحقيقة لا يختص لزومُهُ بفَرْضِ قدم العلَّةِ أو الطبيعة ، بل يلزمُ أيضاً في فرض حدوثهما .
It would necessarily entail either your pre-eternity or the continuation of your non-existence. And the explanation of the entailment: that if the agent does not have will, then if the existence of the possible is necessary due to its [the agent's] existence or due to some attribute of His, such that it does not need intention for the existence of that possible, then the pre-eternity of your essence and the pre-eternity of all possible things would be necessary, because it is impossible for the dependent to exist without that upon which it depends, and the necessity of pre-eternity for your agent and His attributes has already passed, so what is dependent upon them must also be such. And if the existence of the possible is not necessary due to the existence of His essence nor due to the existence of some attribute of His, then the continuation of the non-existence of your essence and the non-existence of all possible things would be necessary, because it is impossible to prefer a time, amount, or attribute without a preferential agent. [Statement of the impossibility of the Maker being a nature or a cause] And from this you know the impossibility of the Maker being a nature or a necessitating cause. For if it were answered with regard to the delay in the nature by a preventing factor or the absence of a condition, then the non-existence of the eternal or an infinite regress would be necessary, because the discussion would be transferred to that preventing factor and that condition. The indication by 'here' refers to the necessity of your pre-eternity or the continuation of your non-existence; that is, by this necessary consequence it is also argued for the impossibility of the Maker of the world being a nature or a necessitating cause. And you have already known in what has preceded that whoever can perform the action and refrain from it is called 'willful' (mukhtār), and whoever cannot refrain from it – if nothing can prevent him from the action – is called 'cause' ('illah), and if it can be prevented, he is called 'nature' (tabī'ah). And the explanation of the necessity of one of the two matters when the Maker of the world is assumed to be a nature or a cause: that the nature and the cause cannot be other than either pre-eternal or originated. If they are pre-eternal, the pre-eternity of the world would be necessary; because the act of the cause and the nature is by necessity, not by choice, and the pre-eternity of the dependent requires the pre-eternity of its necessary consequence. And you have already known by proof the origination of the world. And if they are originated, they would need a cause or a nature, leading to a circle or infinite regress. And the explanation of that has already preceded in the discussion of your agent being pre-eternal. And the circle and infinite regress are impossible according to what has passed. So the existence of the cause and nature being originated is impossible. Therefore the existence of your essence and the entire world that depends on them is impossible. And the impossible is continuously non-existent. So the continuation of non-existence for your essence and for the entire world has become necessary! And direct observation belies that. The conclusion: that the pre-eternity of the world would be necessary if the cause or nature is assumed pre-eternal, and the continuation of its non-existence if they are assumed originated. And both necessary consequences are false, so the dependent – which is the Maker of the world being a nature or cause – is false. So it is necessarily determined that He is an agent by choice, and that is what is sought. 'And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' [al-Qasas: 68]. And it also becomes necessary on the assumption of the cause or nature being pre-eternal that there exists that which has no end; because the relation of the cause and nature to possible things is a single relation, and possible things are without end, so the existence of all of them at once would be necessary. And this impossibility in reality is not specific to the assumption of the pre-eternity of the cause or nature, but rather it also occurs in the assumption of their origination.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a dense kalam proof that the Creator must be a willful agent (fā'il bil-ikhtiyār) and cannot be a mere nature (ṭabī'ah) or a necessitating cause ('illah mūjibah). The argument proceeds in several stages.

First, the author establishes a conditional argument: if the agent (al-fā'il) does not have will (murīd), then either the existence of possible things follows necessarily from His existence or attribute—in which case they would be pre-eternal along with Him—or it does not follow necessarily, in which case their existence would require a preferential factor (murajjiḥ) without which they would never exist. Since an infinite regress of such preferences is impossible, their non-existence would continue forever. Thus, the lack of will leads to absurd consequences.

Second, the author applies this to the specific question of the Maker: from the same necessary consequence (luzūm) we learn that the Maker cannot be a nature or a necessitating cause. An objection is considered: what if the delay in nature's effect is due to a preventing factor (māni') or absence of condition (fawt al-shart)? The answer is that this would either require the non-existence of the eternal (if the preventing factor is eternal) or lead to an infinite regress (if the preventing factor requires another preventing factor, etc.), both of which are impossible.

Third, definitions are given: a willful agent is one from whom both action and refraining are possible; a cause is one from whom refraining is impossible and no preventing factor exists; a nature is one from whom refraining is impossible but a preventing factor could hinder the act.

Fourth, the argument is restated specifically for nature and cause. They must be either pre-eternal or originated. If pre-eternal, the world must also be pre-eternal (which contradicts the proof of the world's origination). If originated, they would need another cause or nature, leading to a circle or infinite regress. Both are false, so the assumption that the Creator is a nature or cause is false. Therefore, He must be a willful agent.

Finally, an additional necessary consequence is drawn: if the cause or nature were pre-eternal, then all possible things (which are infinite) would have to exist simultaneously, which is impossible. This impossibility also holds if they were originated (since the relation is still single and the possible things are infinite). Thus, the proof is reinforced from multiple angles.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the existence of a possible thing is necessarily entailed by the agent's existence, what follows?
A) The possible is originated.
B) The possible is pre-eternal.
C) The possible never exists.
D) The agent must be willful.

Key Takeaway
An agent without will leads to impossible alternatives, proving that the Creator must possess will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that everything that exists does so only because God willed it, how would your view of your own existence and daily events change? Will you live today with that awareness, or will you forget?
?
The argument shows that without divine will, nothing would exist—not even you. Have you ever genuinely thanked God for willing you into existence? What stops you from feeling that gratitude right now?
?
You depend on God's will every moment. Is there an area of your life where you act as if you are the sole agent? Will you hand that area back to Him today?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
7 min
Why the Creator Cannot Be a Nature or Cause
Sanusi argues against the Creator being a nature or cause, with definitions and objection handling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific arguments against the Creator being a nature or a necessitating cause, including the handling of objection about preventing factors.

Core Idea
A nature or cause would force either the world's pre-eternity or an infinite regress.

In this passage, Sanusi directly tackles the alternative views that the Creator might be a blind nature or a necessitating cause. He uses the previous dilemma to show that such views lead to contradictions. He also defines these terms and addresses a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Sanusi defines three types: willful agent (can act or refrain), cause (cannot refrain, no prevention), nature (cannot refrain but can be prevented). Understand these carefully.
These definitions are critical for the argument.
2
Understand the objection and refutation
An objector might say a nature's delay is due to a preventing factor. Sanusi refutes by showing that the preventing factor itself needs explanation, leading to regress.
3
Follow the main argument
Sanusi argues that a nature or cause must be either pre-eternal or originated. Both lead to false consequences: pre-eternity of the world or infinite regress. Thus the assumption is false.

Commentary
This passage is a dense kalam proof that the Creator must be a willful agent (fā'il bil-ikhtiyār) and cannot be a mere nature (ṭabī'ah) or a necessitating cause ('illah mūjibah). The argument proceeds in several stages.

Since an infinite regress of such preferences is impossible, their non-existence would continue forever. Thus, the lack of will leads to absurd consequences.

Second, the author applies this to the specific question of the Maker: from the same necessary consequence (luzūm) we learn that the Maker cannot be a nature or a necessitating cause. An objection is considered: what if the delay in nature's effect is due to a preventing factor (māni') or absence of condition (fawt al-shart)? The answer is that this would either require the non-existence of the eternal (if the preventing factor is eternal) or lead to an infinite regress (if the preventing factor requires another preventing factor, etc.), both of which are impossible.

Third, definitions are given: a willful agent is one from whom both action and refraining are possible; a cause is one from whom refraining is impossible and no preventing factor exists; a nature is one from whom refraining is impossible but a preventing factor could hinder the act.

Fourth, the argument is restated specifically for nature and cause. They must be either pre-eternal or originated. If pre-eternal, the world must also be pre-eternal (which contradicts the proof of the world's origination). If originated, they would need another cause or nature, leading to a circle or infinite regress. Both are false, so the assumption that the Creator is a nature or cause is false. Therefore, He must be a willful agent.

Finally, an additional necessary consequence is drawn: if the cause or nature were pre-eternal, then all possible things (which are infinite) would have to exist simultaneously, which is impossible. This impossibility also holds if they were originated (since the relation is still single and the possible things are infinite). Thus, the proof is reinforced from multiple angles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a nature could produce temporal effects if prevented by a factor.
Correction: The passage shows this leads to an infinite regress of preventing factors.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the nature or cause is originated, what follows?
A) The world is pre-eternal.
B) It leads to a circle or infinite regress.
C) The world exists by chance.
D) The Creator must be willful.

Key Takeaway
The Maker cannot be a nature or cause because that would lead to either the pre-eternity of the world or an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if Allah is a willful agent who chooses when and how to respond to your duas, or do you sometimes act as if He is a mechanical cause that must give you what you want? How does the distinction in this passage challenge your daily supplication?
?
If you believe that nothing can prevent Allah from acting, why do you sometimes feel that your sins or circumstances block His mercy? What does this lesson teach about the nature of prevention, and how might you change your perspective on repentance?
?
The passage argues that if the Creator were a cause, the world would be pre-eternal. Do you internalize that the world is contingent and could vanish at any moment? How would your attachment to worldly things change if you truly believed this?

The Conclusion: Creator Is a Willful Agent
Concluding summary with additional argument against cause/nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final conclusion that the Creator must be a willful agent, and the additional argument from the impossibility of infinite simultaneous existents.

Core Idea
The only rational conclusion is that the Creator is a willful agent.

In this concluding passage, Sanusi draws the inevitable conclusion: since both alternatives for a nature/cause are false, the Creator must be an agent with free choice. He further strengthens the proof by showing that a necessitating cause would require all possible things to exist at once, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the conclusion
Sanusi summarizes the dilemma: if nature/cause pre-eternal, world pre-eternal; if originated, world never exists. Both false, so assumption false.
Note the Quranic verse supporting the conclusion.
2
Understand the additional argument
Sanusi adds that a cause or nature has a single relation to all possible things; since possible things are infinite, they would all have to exist simultaneously, which is impossible.
This argument reinforces the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the overall proof
Consider how the entire argument chain establishes that the Creator must be willful, consistent with Islamic theology.

والعِيانُ يُكَذِّبُ ذلك . والحاصلُ : أَنَّهُ يلزمُ قدمُ العالمِ إِنْ فُرِضَتِ العَلَّةُ أَوِ الطبيعةُ قديمتين ، واستمرار عدمِهِ إِنْ فُرضتا حادثتين ، وكلا اللازمين باطل فالملزوم - وهو كون صانع العالم طبيعة أو عِلَّة - باطل ، فتعيَّنَ أَنْ يكون فاعلاً بالاختيار ، وهو المطلوب ، وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ ويختارُ [القصص : ٦٨] . ويلزم أيضاً على تقديرِ العلَّةِ أو الطبيعة قديمتين وجود ما لا نهاية له ؛ لأنَّ نسبة العلّة والطبيعة إلى الممكنات نسبة واحدة ، والممكناتُ لا نهاية لها ، فيلزم وجود جميعها دفعةً ، وهذا المحالُ في الحقيقة لا يختص لزومُهُ بفَرْضِ قدم العلَّةِ أو الطبيعة ، بل يلزمُ أيضاً في فرض حدوثهما .
It would necessarily entail either your pre-eternity or the continuation of your non-existence. And the explanation of the entailment: that if the agent does not have will, then if the existence of the possible is necessary due to its [the agent's] existence or due to some attribute of His, such that it does not need intention for the existence of that possible, then the pre-eternity of your essence and the pre-eternity of all possible things would be necessary, because it is impossible for the dependent to exist without that upon which it depends, and the necessity of pre-eternity for your agent and His attributes has already passed, so what is dependent upon them must also be such. And if the existence of the possible is not necessary due to the existence of His essence nor due to the existence of some attribute of His, then the continuation of the non-existence of your essence and the non-existence of all possible things would be necessary, because it is impossible to prefer a time, amount, or attribute without a preferential agent. [Statement of the impossibility of the Maker being a nature or a cause] And from this you know the impossibility of the Maker being a nature or a necessitating cause. For if it were answered with regard to the delay in the nature by a preventing factor or the absence of a condition, then the non-existence of the eternal or an infinite regress would be necessary, because the discussion would be transferred to that preventing factor and that condition. The indication by 'here' refers to the necessity of your pre-eternity or the continuation of your non-existence; that is, by this necessary consequence it is also argued for the impossibility of the Maker of the world being a nature or a necessitating cause. And you have already known in what has preceded that whoever can perform the action and refrain from it is called 'willful' (mukhtār), and whoever cannot refrain from it – if nothing can prevent him from the action – is called 'cause' ('illah), and if it can be prevented, he is called 'nature' (tabī'ah). And the explanation of the necessity of one of the two matters when the Maker of the world is assumed to be a nature or a cause: that the nature and the cause cannot be other than either pre-eternal or originated. If they are pre-eternal, the pre-eternity of the world would be necessary; because the act of the cause and the nature is by necessity, not by choice, and the pre-eternity of the dependent requires the pre-eternity of its necessary consequence. And you have already known by proof the origination of the world. And if they are originated, they would need a cause or a nature, leading to a circle or infinite regress. And the explanation of that has already preceded in the discussion of your agent being pre-eternal. And the circle and infinite regress are impossible according to what has passed. So the existence of the cause and nature being originated is impossible. Therefore the existence of your essence and the entire world that depends on them is impossible. And the impossible is continuously non-existent. So the continuation of non-existence for your essence and for the entire world has become necessary! And direct observation belies that. The conclusion: that the pre-eternity of the world would be necessary if the cause or nature is assumed pre-eternal, and the continuation of its non-existence if they are assumed originated. And both necessary consequences are false, so the dependent – which is the Maker of the world being a nature or cause – is false. So it is necessarily determined that He is an agent by choice, and that is what is sought. 'And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' [al-Qasas: 68]. And it also becomes necessary on the assumption of the cause or nature being pre-eternal that there exists that which has no end; because the relation of the cause and nature to possible things is a single relation, and possible things are without end, so the existence of all of them at once would be necessary. And this impossibility in reality is not specific to the assumption of the pre-eternity of the cause or nature, but rather it also occurs in the assumption of their origination.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a dense kalam proof that the Creator must be a willful agent (fā'il bil-ikhtiyār) and cannot be a mere nature (ṭabī'ah) or a necessitating cause ('illah mūjibah). The argument proceeds in several stages.

First, the author establishes a conditional argument: if the agent (al-fā'il) does not have will (murīd), then either the existence of possible things follows necessarily from His existence or attribute—in which case they would be pre-eternal along with Him—or it does not follow necessarily, in which case their existence would require a preferential factor (murajjiḥ) without which they would never exist. Since an infinite regress of such preferences is impossible, their non-existence would continue forever. Thus, the lack of will leads to absurd consequences.

Second, the author applies this to the specific question of the Maker: from the same necessary consequence (luzūm) we learn that the Maker cannot be a nature or a necessitating cause. An objection is considered: what if the delay in nature's effect is due to a preventing factor (māni') or absence of condition (fawt al-shart)? The answer is that this would either require the non-existence of the eternal (if the preventing factor is eternal) or lead to an infinite regress (if the preventing factor requires another preventing factor, etc.), both of which are impossible.

Third, definitions are given: a willful agent is one from whom both action and refraining are possible; a cause is one from whom refraining is impossible and no preventing factor exists; a nature is one from whom refraining is impossible but a preventing factor could hinder the act.

Fourth, the argument is restated specifically for nature and cause. They must be either pre-eternal or originated. If pre-eternal, the world must also be pre-eternal (which contradicts the proof of the world's origination). If originated, they would need another cause or nature, leading to a circle or infinite regress. Both are false, so the assumption that the Creator is a nature or cause is false. Therefore, He must be a willful agent.

Finally, an additional necessary consequence is drawn: if the cause or nature were pre-eternal, then all possible things (which are infinite) would have to exist simultaneously, which is impossible. This impossibility also holds if they were originated (since the relation is still single and the possible things are infinite). Thus, the proof is reinforced from multiple angles.

Key Terms
فاعل بالاختيار
مطلوب
ما لا نهاية له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if the cause is pre-eternal, only a finite set of possible things exist.
Correction: The passage argues that the relation is uniform and thus all possible things must exist at once.

Checkpoint
What additional argument does Sanusi provide against the Creator being a cause or nature?
A) The world is too complex.
B) The cause would produce all possible things at once, leading to an impossibility.
C) The cause would require a second cause.
D) The cause would be temporal.

Key Takeaway
The Creator must be a willful agent, as the only coherent explanation for the existence of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever live as if God is a blind, impersonal force—expecting automatic outcomes from your actions without turning to His will? How can you personally acknowledge Him as the willful Agent who 'creates what He wills and chooses' in your decisions today?
?
The passage argues that a cause or nature would produce all possible things at once, yet we see creation unfolding in time. What does this tell you about your own need to submit to a personal God who freely chooses to create in a specific order? Are you living in patient trust of His timing, or demanding instant satisfaction?
?
If your relationship with the Creator is with a personal, willful Agent, does your prayer reflect a conversation with a free, choosing Lord, or is it merely a ritual? What one concrete change will you make in your prayers today to acknowledge that He is al-Fāʿil bi-al-Ikhtiyār?

The Naturalists' Objection: Delayed Effects and Sanusi's Reply
Sanusi presents the naturalists' objection and begins his answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the naturalists' argument that an eternal nature can have delayed effects due to obstacles and conditions, and how Sanusi begins to refute it by examining the obstacle or condition.

Core Idea
Obstacles and conditions don't save the nature hypothesis — they push the problem back one step.

The naturalists propose that the Maker of the universe is an eternal nature, like fire, but that its effect (the world) can be delayed if obstacles or missing conditions prevent it. They use this to avoid the conclusion that the world must be eternal. Sanusi's response is to ask about the obstacle or condition itself: is it eternal or originated? This moves the argument to a new level.

Guided Path
1
Understand the naturalists' objection
Read the passage to grasp the naturalists' claim: the Maker is an eternal nature, but the world's creation was delayed due to obstacles or missing conditions.
Notice that they grant the Maker is a nature, but deny the effect must be simultaneous.
2
Examine their example of fire
The naturalists use the example of fire: by its nature it burns, but only if it contacts something burnable and there is no moisture. This illustrates how an effect can be delayed.
The example is meant to show that a cause can exist without its effect temporarily.
3
See Sanusi's initial response
Sanusi shifts the discussion to the obstacle or condition itself. He argues that this obstacle/condition must be either eternal or originated, leading to a new dilemma.
This is the beginning of a refutation that will be continued.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فإنْ أُجيب عن التأخُرِ في الطبيعة . . . ) إلى آخره : هذا اعتراضُ مِنَ الطبائعيِّينَ على الدليل السابق ؛ وهو لزوم قدم العالم أو استمرار عدمه، وتقريرُهُ : أن قالوا : نختارُ أنَّ صانع الحوادثِ طبيعة ، وأنَّها قديمةٌ ، قولكم : ( فيلزمُ قدم تلك الحوادثِ ) غيرُ مسلَّم ؛ لأنَّ عدم المفارقةِ إنَّما يلزمُ في العلة مع معلولها لأنَّ تلازمهما لا يتوقَّفُ على شيء ، أما ملازمة الطبيعة مطبوعها فمتوقف على عدمِ الموانع ووجودِ الشرائط ، كما نقولُ مثلاً : تأثير النارِ بطبعها على مذهب الفلاسفة - أبعدهم الله - في احتراق الشيء .. يتوقف على وجود شرط ؛ وهو مسُّها مثلاً لذلك المحترق ، وانتفاء مانع ؛ وهو بلل ذلك الممسوس مثلاً ، أما إذا وُجِدَ مانعُها أو انتفى شرطُها فتُوجَدُ هي مع عدم مطبوعها الذي هو الاحتراق . قالوا : فإذا تقرَّرَ هذا فنقولُ : صانعُ هذه الحوادثِ طبيعةٌ قديمة ، لكن تأخَّرَ مطبوعها ولم يكن قديماً لمانع من وجودِهِ أزلاً أو فوات شرط ، فلما انتفى المانع ووُجِدَ الشرط فيما لا يزالُ . . وُجِدَتْ تلك الحوادث ، فلا يلزم على هذا قدمُ الحوادثِ ولا استمرار عدمها كما زعمتم . وجوابُهُ : أنَّا ننقلُ الكلام معهم إلى ذلك المانع مِنْ وجودِ الحوادثِ أوِ الشرط لها المتأخر وجودُهُ ، فنقولُ : ذلك المانع مِنْ تأثير الطبيعة في وجود الحوادث أزلاً لا يخلو : إما أن تُقدروه قديماً أو حادثاً :
His statement: 'If it is answered concerning the delay in the nature...' to the end: this is an objection from the naturalists against the previous proof—which is the necessity of the eternity of the world or its perpetual non-existence. Its presentation: they said: We choose that the Maker of events is a nature, and that it is eternal. Your statement: 'So the eternality of those events follows' is not conceded, because the lack of separation only follows in the case of a cause with its effect, since their inseparability does not depend on anything. As for the inseparability of a nature from its impressed effect (matbu'), it depends on the absence of obstacles and the presence of conditions. For example, the effect of fire by its nature, according to the philosophers—may Allah distance them—in burning something depends on the existence of a condition—such as contact with the burning thing—and the absence of an obstacle—such as moisture on that touched thing. If its obstacle exists or its condition is absent, then the nature exists without its impressed effect, which is burning. They said: So when this is established, we say: The Maker of these events is an eternal nature, but its impressed effect was delayed and was not eternal due to an obstacle to its existence eternally or a missing condition. So when the obstacle was removed and the condition came to be in what is yet to come, those events came to be. Thus, neither the eternality of the events nor the perpetual non-existence of them follows as you claimed. And his answer: We transfer the discussion with them to that obstacle to the existence of events or the condition whose existence was delayed, and we say: That obstacle to the nature's influence in bringing about events eternally is not free: either you assume it eternal or originated:
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the naturalists (al-taba'i'iyyun) against Sanusi's previous proof that the Maker cannot be a nature. They grant that the Maker is an eternal nature, but deny that the world must therefore be eternal. They argue that a nature produces its effect only when obstacles are absent and conditions are present. They give the example of fire: by its nature it burns, but only if it contacts a burnable object and there is no moisture to prevent burning. Thus, an eternal nature can exist without its effect if an obstacle prevents it or a condition is absent. So they claim that the world's events were delayed because of an obstacle or missing condition, and when the obstacle was removed and the condition present, the events occurred. Therefore, neither the world's eternity nor its perpetual non-existence follows. Sanusi begins his answer by moving the discussion to the obstacle or condition itself: that obstacle or condition must be either eternal or originated. This sets up a new dilemma. The answer is incomplete at this page, but we see the line of refutation: the obstacle or condition will itself need explanation, leading back to the same issues.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that invoking obstacles and conditions saves the nature hypothesis from the need for simultaneous effect.
Correction: Sanusi shows that it merely pushes the problem back: the obstacle or condition itself requires explanation, leading back to the same dilemma.

Checkpoint
What is the naturalists' objection regarding the delay of a nature's effect?
A) The nature itself can be delayed by its own will.
B) The effect is delayed due to obstacles or missing conditions.
C) The nature is not eternal, so the effect can be delayed.
D) The cause and effect are inseparable regardless of obstacles.

Key Takeaway
The naturalists' attempt to avoid the eternity of the world by appealing to obstacles and conditions fails because the obstacle or condition itself requires explanation.

?

How does this lesson's exposure of the weakness of nature as an explanation change how you view everyday events? Will you consciously remind yourself of Allah's direct involvement today?
?
Consider a recent trial or obstacle you faced. Did you see it as a mere consequence of natural circumstances, or did you reflect on the deeper cause in Allah's decree? This lesson teaches that obstacles themselves must have a cause—can you trace that obstacle back to a test from Allah? How will you respond to the next obstacle with this awareness?
?
The naturalists' error is to assume that a nature can act without a voluntary will behind it. In your own life, where have you unconsciously relied on 'second causes' while neglecting the first cause—Allah's will? What concrete step can you take today to shift your reliance from natural expectations to direct trust in Allah?

Refuting Philosophers Using the Spheres
Sanusi presents a refutation of philosophers using the specific number and magnitudes of the spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the specific number and sizes of the celestial spheres serve as evidence for Allah's will and choice against the philosophers' theory of necessary causation.

Core Idea
The precise details of creation prove it is the result of choice, not necessity.

This passage introduces a key kalam argument against the philosophers who claim the universe is a necessary emanation. By pointing to the specific number and sizes of the celestial spheres, the author shows that these contingent features must be the result of a free and wise choice, not blind necessity. Let's examine how this simple observation dismantles the deterministic worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to grasp the argument's structure. Notice the rhetorical question that forms the core of the refutation.
2
Identify the philosophers' position
The philosophers affirm causation (ta'lil) and deny choice (ikhtiyar) and will (irada) to the Creator. Understand what they believe before seeing how it is refuted.
3
Analyze the rhetorical question
The author asks why the spheres have a specific number and specific magnitudes. Note that these could have been otherwise, which is the key to the argument.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The contingency of these features implies a free chooser who decided on these specific values. This undermines the idea of a necessary universe.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider how this argument points to a voluntary Creator and how it fits into broader kalam arguments for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يبطل مذهب الفلاسفة القائلين بالتعليل، النافين عن الصانع الاختيار والإرادة: أن يقال لهم: ما بال الأفلاك وقفت على عدد مخصوص ولم تكن أكثر منه ولا أقل؟ ولم كانت على تلك المقادير المخصوصة ولم تكن أكبر منها
And among what invalidates the doctrine of the philosophers who affirm causation, and who deny the Creator choice and will, is that it is said to them: Why did the spheres stop at a specific number, being neither more nor less? And why were they at those specific magnitudes and not larger?
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage introduces a new refutation of the philosophers (falasifa) who believe in necessary causation (ta'lil) and deny that the Creator has choice (ikhtiyar) and will (irada). The author presents a simple but powerful argument: the spheres (the celestial bodies) have a specific number (e.g., nine or ten in classical astronomy) and specific sizes. If the universe were a necessary emanation from a cause, there would be no reason why the number or sizes are exactly as they are—they could have been more or less, larger or smaller. But the fact that they are precisely determined shows that they are the result of a free choice by a wise agent, not a blind necessity. This argument is a staple of kalam theology against Aristotelian cosmology. The passage is incomplete but the direction is clear.

B) Their specific number and magnitudes
C) Their motion and direction
D) Their supposed eternity

Key Takeaway
The specific contingent features of the universe, like the number and sizes of the spheres, point to a Creator with free will and choice, not a necessary cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The philosophers denied God's choice because they thought it beneath Him. Do you ever act as if God's will is mechanical—as if He is obligated to grant your requests or follow your expectations? How can you repent from that presumption today?

The Specificity of the Cosmos Points to a Free Creator
Sanusi poses rhetorical questions about celestial motions and positions to argue for a free Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingent specificities of the celestial spheres and stars refute the philosophers' doctrine of necessary causation and prove a free Creator.

Core Idea
Every specific detail of the cosmos that could be otherwise requires a free Chooser.

Sanusi now moves from conceptual arguments to concrete physical examples. He poses a series of pointed questions about the precise features of the heavens — the number of spheres, their motions, the positions of stars — that on the philosophers' own principle of necessary causation have no explanation. This demonstrates that these features must be the result of a free and wise Creator who chooses and specifies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the questions
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to each specific feature Sanusi asks about: the number of spheres, their sizes, motions, etc.
The passage is a single continuous argument; try to see the pattern.
2
Understand the logical structure
Each question highlights a contingent feature that could have been otherwise. Sanusi argues that on the philosophers' principle of necessary causation, there is no reason for these specific details.
The key is that if causation were necessary, the cosmos would have no room for such specific choices.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The impossibility of explaining these specificities by necessary causation forces us to accept a free Agent who specifies as He wills.
Sanusi ends with a harsh dismissal of the opposing view and a supplication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يبطل مذهب الفلاسفة القائلين بالتعليل، النافينَ عنِ الصانع الاختيار والإرادةَ: أَنْ يُقالَ لهم: ما بال الأفلاكِ وقفَتْ على عددٍ مخصوص ولم تكن أكثرَ منهُ ولا أقل؟ ولِمَ كانَتْ على تلك المقادير المخصوصة ولم تكن أكبر منها ولا أصغر؟ وما بالُ الأعلى منها يتحرَّكُ حركةً واحدةً مِنَ المشرقِ إلى المغرب، وباقي الأفلاك تتحرَّكُ حركتين؛ إحداهما: الحركة اليومية مِنَ المشرقِ إلى المغرب، والأخرى: حركاتها في البروج مِنَ المغرب إلى المشرقِ؟ وما بالُ الحركاتِ كلّها اختصت بما بين المشرق والمغرب، ولم تكن فيما بين الجنوب والشِّمالِ مثلاً؟ ولِمَ اختص كلُّ واحدٍ مِنَ السبعةِ السَّيَّارَةِ بفَلَكِهِ المخصوص معَ جواز أنْ يكونَ في غيرِهِ؟ ولِمَ اختصَّتْ سائر الكواكب الثابتة بالفلكِ الثامن ولم تكن في غيرِهِ؟ ولِمَ كانَ الفلك التاسع أطلس مِنَ الكواكب؟(1)، ولِمَ كانَ بعض الكواكب أكبرَ مِنْ بعض؟ ولِمَ كَانَ بعضُها يلي القطبَ الشَّماليَّ، وبعضُها يلي القطب الجنوبي، وبعضُها على سمتِ الرؤوس، وبعضُها مائلاً عنه؟ ولا مُوجِبَ للتخصيص بجميع ما ذُكِرَ على أصلكم. وهل مذهبكم في إسنادِ ذلك إلى غيرِ الفاعل المختار الذي يخص ما شاءَ بما شاءَ إلا تلاعب لا يرضى بقوله إلا مسلوب العقل والإيمان، ومَنْ لم ينفعهُ اللهُ بشيءٍ ممَّا تعبَ في تعلّمِهِ، وصار يهذو هذيان المجانينِ وغيرِ المميَّرِ مِنَ الصبيانِ، ولا حول ولا قُوَّةَ إلا باللهِ؟! اللهم؛ عافنا بفضلِكَ مِنْ كلّ آفةٍ في ديننا ودنيانا وآخرتنا يا أرحم الراحمينَ، يا ذا الجلال والإكرام. (1) أطلس : أسود ، وقوله : ( من الكواكب ) أي : من أجل خلوه منها ، ففي الكلام حذف ، أو ضمن ( أطلس ) معنى : متجرّد أو عارٍ ، فعدى بـ ( من ) . حامدي » ( ص ۱۸۳ )
And among that which invalidates the doctrine of the philosophers who adhere to causation, and who deny the Creator choice and will, is that it is said to them: What is it that made the spheres stop at a specific number, neither more nor less? And why were they at those specific magnitudes, neither larger nor smaller? And what is it that makes the highest of them move with a single motion from east to west, while the rest of the spheres move with two motions: one of which is the daily motion from east to west, and the other is their motion through the zodiac from west to east? And what makes all the motions restricted to between east and west, and not between south and north, for example? And why was each of the seven planets restricted to its own specific sphere, when it is possible for it to be in another? And why were the rest of the fixed stars restricted to the eighth sphere and not in another? And why was the ninth sphere devoid of stars? And why are some stars larger than others? And why are some of them near the northern pole, some near the southern pole, some at the zenith, and some inclined away from it? There is no cause for specification in all that has been mentioned according to your principle. And is your doctrine of attributing that to other than the free Agent who specifies whatever He wills with whatever He wills anything but a frivolity whose statement is accepted only by one who is deprived of intellect and faith, and one whom God has not benefited with anything of what he has labored to learn, and who became delirious like the delirium of madmen and the indiscriminate of children? And there is no power nor strength except through God! O God, grant us by Your grace from every affliction in our religion, our world, and our afterlife, O Most Merciful of the merciful, O Possessor of majesty and honor. (1) Atlas: black, and His saying: (of the stars) i.e., because of its emptiness from them, so there is an ellipsis in the speech, or (atlas) has the meaning of stripped or bare, so it is transitive with 'min'. Hamidi (p. 183)
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a series of rhetorical questions posed to the philosophers who believe in necessary causation (al-ta'lil) and deny divine choice and will. The author begins by stating that these questions invalidate their doctrine. He then enumerates specific contingent features of the cosmos: the number and sizes of the celestial spheres, the motions of the highest sphere versus the others (single vs. double motions), the restriction of motions to the east-west direction, the assignment of each planet to its own sphere, the fixed stars in the eighth sphere, the emptiness of the ninth sphere, the varying sizes of stars, and their positions relative to the poles and zenith. He argues that on the philosophers' principle of necessary causation, there is no cause that compels these specific details; they could have been otherwise. Therefore, these specificities point to a free Agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) who chooses and specifies. He then concludes harshly that attributing these to anything other than the free Agent is mere frivolity, accepted only by someone devoid of intellect and faith. The passage ends with a supplication to God for protection.

تعليل (causation/ta'lil)
فاعل مختار (free Agent)
تخصيص (specification)
الأفلاك (spheres)
الكواكب السيارة (planets)
الكواكب الثابتة (fixed stars)
أطلس (atlas/devoid)
الحركة اليومية (daily motion)
البروج (zodiac)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The specific details of the cosmos (number of spheres, motions, star positions) can be explained by natural laws or chance alone, without a Creator.
Correction: Sanusi argues that these specificities are contingent and have no necessary cause; they could have been otherwise, so they require a free Agent who chooses.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the series of questions about the celestial spheres and stars?
A) To show that the philosophers do not know astronomy.
B) To demonstrate that these specific details have no cause on the philosophers' own principle, thus requiring a free Agent.
C) To prove that the spheres are eternal.
D) To argue that the planets move by accident.

Key Takeaway
The contingent specificities of the cosmos cannot be explained by necessary causation and thus demand a free Creator who chooses and specifies as He wills.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the precision of the cosmos—its size, motions, and your own place in it—do you take it for granted, or does it strike you that every detail could have been otherwise? What in your life have you been attributing to 'just how things work' rather than to the deliberate choice of a free Creator? Will you pause today to thank Him for choosing for you the world and circumstances you have?
?
Sanusi accuses those who deny divine choice of being like those 'deprived of intellect and faith.' Where in your own thinking might you be subtly denying God's freedom—perhaps by assuming that your circumstances, your traits, or your struggles are fixed by blind forces rather than by His wise choice? What concrete step can you take today to surrender that area back to His will?
?
The philosophers' error was to see the universe as necessary, leaving no room for a choosing God. Do you ever live as if your life's details are dictated by necessity—your habits, your past, your limitations—rather than seeing each moment as a fresh act of divine specification? How would your next decision change if you truly believed that the God who chose every star's position also chooses your path?

Proof of God's Knowledge from Perfect Design
Proof that God must be knowing from order in creation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the perfect design of the human body is used in kalam to prove that God is knowing (Alim).

Core Idea
Perfect order in creation proves a knowing Creator.

In this passage, the commentator presents a classic teleological argument for God's knowledge. He focuses on the human body as a clear example of intricate craftsmanship, where each part is perfectly suited to its function. The argument is structured as a conditional proof: if the Maker lacked knowledge, such perfection could not exist. This lesson will guide you through the proof up to the point where the necessary connection is established.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal English translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
The passage ends abruptly due to a page break.
2
Identify the main claim and examples
The commentator asserts that the Maker must be knowing. He gives examples from the human body: each part specializes in a particular benefit and is provided with what preserves it.
3
Understand the conditional structure
The proof is formalized as a conditional: 'if your Maker were not knowing, you would not be characterized by these perfections.' This is a logical implication.
Notice the use of 'law' (if) to set up the conditional.
4
Grasp the intuitive necessary connection
The necessary connection (mulazama) is explained as known by intuition (badiha): an action is not perfected and brought forth except through knowledge. This is a self-evident principle.
This step is where the passage cuts off.
5
Reflect on the incompleteness
Note that the argument is incomplete due to the page break. However, the structure up to this point shows a clear proof: perfection requires a knowing agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يجب أيضاً لصانعِكَ أنْ يكون عالماً ، وإلا لم تكن على ما أنتَ عليهِ مِنْ دقائقِ الصنع ؛ في اختصاص كلّ جزء منكَ بمنفعتِهِ الخاصَّةِ بهِ ، وإمداده بما يحفظها عليه ، ونحو ذلكَ مِنَ المحاسنِ التي تعجز عقول البشر عن الإحاطة بأسرارها . نظم الدليل على لفظِهِ أنْ يُقالَ : لو لم يكن صانعُكَ عالماً لم تكن متصفاً بما أنتَ عليهِ مِنْ غاية الإحكام ودقائقِ المحاسنِ التي يُعجَزُ عن حصرها . وبيان الملازمة : أنَّه معلوم بالبديهة أنَّهُ لا يُحكم الفعل ويُبرِزُهُ في
Then it is also necessary that your Maker be knowing. Otherwise, you would not be as you are of the intricacies of craftsmanship: in the specialization of each part of you for its particular benefit, and its provision with what preserves it, and similar perfections that the minds of humans are incapable of encompassing in their secrets. The proof is formalized in its wording as follows: if your Maker were not knowing, you would not be characterized by what you are upon of utmost perfection and intricate perfections that are impossible to enumerate. The explanation of the necessary connection is that it is known by intuition that an action is not perfected and brought forth in...
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God must possess the attribute of knowledge (al-ilm). The author begins by stating that it is necessary (wajib) for the Maker (as-sani') to be knowing, because otherwise the intricate design of the human body would not exist. He gives examples: each organ is specialized for a specific function and is supplied with what preserves it—these are perfections that human minds cannot fully grasp. Then he formalizes the proof as a conditional syllogism (lu… law): if the Maker were not knowing, you would not possess these perfections. The necessary connection (mulazama) between not-knowing and the absence of perfection is explained as an intuitive truth (badiha): no action can be perfected and brought forth (presumably 'in a perfect manner') except by a knowledgeable agent. The argument is cut off at the end of the page, but the structure is clear.

Correction: This proof specifically targets the attribute of knowledge: the intricate design of the human body requires a knowing designer, not just a powerful one.
